الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن
وأخرجه الدارقطني عن إسحاق بن أبي إسرائيل عن حماد بن زيد عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن ثعلبة بن صغير أو عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن صغير عن أبيه.
وأخرجه أيضاً عن يزيد بن هارون عن حماد بن زيد عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن صغير أو ثعلبة عن أبيه.
وأخرجه أيضاً عن سليمان بن حرب عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير عن أبيه.
وأخرجه أيضاً عن مسدد عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن ابن أبي صعير عن أبيه.
وأخرجه البيهقي عن مسدد عن حماد بن زيد عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير عن أبيه.
وأخرجه أيضاً عن سليمان بن داود ومسدد عن حماد بن زيد عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري وفي رواية سليمان بن داود عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة وثعلبة بن عبد الله بن أبي صغير عن أبيه.
والحديث رواه غير النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري وحصل فيه الاختلاف عينه في اسم راويه.
فقد أخرجه البخاري وأبو داود وابن أبي عاصم وابن خزيمة والطحاوي وابن قانع والطبراني والحاكم وابن الأثير من طريق بكر بن وائل عن الزهري عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن صغير عن أبيه.
وأخرجه أبو نعيم وابن حزم من طريق بكر بن وائل عن الزهري عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن أبي ضعير عن أبيه.
وأخرجه أبو نعيم من طريق بحر السقاء عن الزهري عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة ابن صعير عن أبيه.
ثم إن الحديث قد اختلف فيه اختلافاً غير هذا واضطرب في إسناده فقد أخرجه الدارقطني من طريق سفيان بن عيينة عن الزهري عن ابن أبي صغير عن أبي هريرة به.
وأخرجه عبد الرزاق وأحمد والبخاري والطحاوي في شرح المعاني والدارقطني والبيهقي عن معمر عن الزهري عن الأعرج عن أبي هريرة به موقوفاً ثم قال يعني معمراً وبلغني أن الزهري كان يرويه إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم.

Al-Daraqutni narrated it from Ishaq ibn Abi Isra'il from Hammad ibn Zaid from al-Nu'man ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri from Tha'laba ibn Saghir or 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Saghir from his father. And al-Bayhaqi narrated it from Musaddad from Hammad ibn Zaid from al-Nu'man ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri from Tha'laba ibn Abi Sa'ir from his father. And he also narrated it from Sulayman ibn Dawud and Musaddad from Hammad ibn Zaid from al-Nu'man ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri — and in the narration of Sulayman ibn Dawud: from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba and Tha'laba ibn 'Abdullah ibn Abi Saghir from his father. Indeed, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Ibn Abi 'Asim, Ibn Khuzaymah, al-Tahawi, Ibn Qani', al-Tabarani, al-Hakim, and Ibn al-Athir narrated it via Bakr ibn Wail from al-Zuhri from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Saghir from his father. And Abu Nu'aym narrated it via Bahr al-Saqqa' from al-Zuhri from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Su'ayr from his father. Then, the hadith had other differences and confusion in its chain; al-Daraqutni narrated it via Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah from al-Zuhri from Ibn Abi Saghir from Abu Hurayrah. And 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Bukhari, al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi narrated it from Ma'mar from al-Zuhri from al-A'raj from Abu Hurayrah as a mawquf narration, then Ma'mar said: 'It reached me that al-Zuhri used to narrate it to the Prophet (peace be upon him).' And al-Daraqutni narrated it via Sulayman ibn Arqam from al-Zuhri from...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed case study from the chapter on differences in chain and text (sanad and matn). The author presents multiple chains for a hadith narrated by al-Zuhri, highlighting how the name of the companion (Tha'laba versus his son 'Abdullah) varies across transmissions. The purpose is to show that such chain variations are a source of ikhtilaf among scholars, affecting the identification of the narrator and potentially the ruling. The passage lists numerous narrators and sources, demonstrating the extent of the discrepancy. In Hanafi usul, such variations are critically examined to determine the most reliable narration. The lesson here is methodological: chain analysis is essential for authenticating hadith and resolving juristic differences.

Key Terms
الاختلاف في السند (al-ikhtilaf fi al-sanad): Variation in the chain of narration, such as different names of narrators or transmission paths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main discrepancy observed in the hadith chains presented?
A) The text of the hadith differs significantly.
B) The name of the companion is reported as either Tha'laba or his son 'Abdullah.
C) The hadith is narrated by different companions.
D) The hadith is mawquf in all narrations.

Key Takeaway
Chain variation is a common feature in hadith transmission; the Hanafi school uses tarjih to resolve such differences and identify the most reliable narration.

Variation in Sanad and Matn: The Zakat al-Fitr Hadith on Wheat
Compiling and analyzing multiple chains and textual variants of the wheat hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the effect of sanad and matn discrepancies on the reliability of a hadith and apply the Hanafi criterion for preferring mursal reports in cases of conflict.

Core Idea
Severe discrepancies in sanad weaken a hadith; the soundest textual variant is determined by tarjih.

This passage compiles multiple chains and textual variants of the hadith on Zakat al-Fitr concerning wheat. You will see how the author collects narrations, identifies weaknesses, and applies principles of tarjih (preference). Understanding this process is essential for a sound usul al-fiqh methodology.

Guided Path
1
List the major sanad variants
Identify the different routes from al-Zuhri and the different Companions or Successors mentioned (e.g., Abu Hurayrah, Zayd ibn Thabit, 'Abdullah ibn Tha'labah, and the mursal from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab).
Pay attention to the names of narrators and whether the report is marfu', mawquf, or mursal.
2
Identify the matn differences
Note the two conflicting amounts mentioned: a full sa' of wheat and half a sa' of wheat.
These differences appear in specific chains; note which chain supports which amount.
3
Apply the Hanafi tarjih principle
The author considers the mursal report from al-Zuhri via Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab as the soundest. Explain why: the mursal has a consistent chain (al-Zuhri from Sa'id) and is preferred over contradictory musnad chains.
Hanafi usul accepts mursal hadith from reliable Successors as hujjah, especially when musnad chains are conflicting.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The preferred ruling is half a sa' of wheat for Zakat al-Fitr, which is the Hanafi position.
This is derived from the tarjih performed by al-Daraqutni and supported by the practice of the Companions as noted by Ibn al-Mundhir.

وابن خزيمة)، والطحاوي ) ، وابن قانع (۳)، والطبراني)، والحاكم)، وابن الأثير)، من طريق بكر بن وائل عن الزهري، عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن صغير، عن أبيه. وأخرجه أبو نعيم ) ، وابن حزم ) من طريق بكر بن وائل، عن الزهري، عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن أبي ضعير، عن أبيه. وأخرجه أبو نعيم من طريق بحر السقاء، عن الزهري، عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة ابن صعير، عن أبيه.
ثم إن الحديث قد اختلف فيه اختلافاً غير هذا واضطرب في إسناده فقد أخرجه الدارقطني من طريق سفيان بن عيينة، عن الزهري، عن ابن أبي صغير، عن أبي هريرة به.

And Ibn Khuzaymah, al-Tahawi, Ibn Qani', al-Tabarani, al-Hakim, Ibn al-Athir, via the route of Bakr ibn Wa'il from al-Zuhri, from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'labah ibn Saghir, from his father. All four: Sufyan, Ibrahim, 'Abd al-Rahman, and 'Uqayl, from al-Zuhri, from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, as a mursal report. This is what I was able to collect of the routes of the hadith, and these severe differences weaken the hadith, indicating the lack of precision of its narrator. The difference was not limited to its sanad; they also differed in its matn. Al-Daraqutni said: They also differed in its matn: in the hadith of Sufyan ibn Husayn from al-Zuhri: a sa' of wheat, and likewise al-Nu'man ibn Rashid said from al-Zuhri from Tha'labah ibn Abi Su'ayr from his father: a sa' of wheat for every person, and in the hadith of others: half a sa' of wheat. The soundest of the two is from al-Zuhri, from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, as a mursal. Ibn al-Mundhir said: We do not know of a reliable report from the Prophet concerning wheat that can be relied upon. Barley was not in Madinah at that time except a little of it. When it became abundant in the time of the Companions, they saw that from it (wheat) the equivalent of a sa' of barley takes its place—and they are the Imams.

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed textual analysis of a hadith related to Zakat al-Fitr, specifically the amount of wheat to be given. The author compiles multiple chains of transmission (sanad) for the hadith, showing significant variation in narrators and routes. These include transmissions from al-Zuhri through various students (Bakr ibn Wa'il, Ma'mar, Ibn Jurayj, Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah, etc.), each with different companions as the ultimate source (Abu Hurayrah, Zayd ibn Thabit, 'Abdullah ibn Tha'labah, and a mursal from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab). The author notes that these severe differences in the sanad weaken the hadith, indicating lack of precision in its narrator. Then the discussion moves to differences in the matn (text): some narrations mention a full sa' of wheat, while others mention half a sa'. Al-Daraqutni declares the soundest version to be the mursal report from al-Zuhri via Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, which likely supports the half-sa' position. Finally, Ibn al-Mundhir states that there is no reliable hadith from the Prophet on wheat, explaining that barley was scarce in Madinah at the time, but later when it became abundant, the Companions equated half a sa' of wheat to a full sa' of barley. This analysis underscores how variations in sanad and matn affect the legal weight of a hadith.

Key Terms
صاع (sa'): A unit of volume measurement used for Zakat al-Fitr, equivalent to approximately 2.5-3 kg of grain.
بر (burr): Wheat (Triticum aestivum). In the context of Zakat al-Fitr, wheat is one of the grains that can be given.
شعير (sha'ir): Barley (Hordeum vulgare). One of the staple grains at the time of the Prophet for Zakat al-Fitr.
مرسل (mursal): A hadith in which the Companion is omitted; the Successor narrates directly from the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
When a hadith has severe discrepancies in both sanad and matn, the soundest variant is determined by careful tarjih, which in this case supports the half-sa' amount for wheat.

The Impact of Sanad and Matn Differences on Fiqh: The Case of Half Sa' of Wheat
Textual analysis of hadith chains and texts leading to fiqh dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the chains and texts of a hadith to identify its weakness and explain how it leads to a valid juristic dispute.

Core Idea
A hadith's legal weight depends on chain reliability and text consistency; when both are weak, jurists rely on companion practice and istihsan.

This passage from 'The Effect of Differences in Chains and Texts on Juristic Differences' demonstrates how a single hadith can generate multiple legal opinions. By examining the hadith on half a sa' of wheat for zakat al-fitr, we see that both its chain (sanad) and text (matn) suffer from variations and weaknesses. Hadith critics like al-Daraqutni and Ibn al-Mundhir question its authenticity, yet the Hanafi school still accepts half a sa' based on companion practice and juristic preference. Understanding this process is essential for usul al-fiqh: you learn to evaluate a hadith's strength and see how jurists navigate weak evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its multiple chains (turuq)
Read the passage and list all the different chains mentioned for the hadith. Note the narrators and whether the chain is mursal, muttasil, etc.
The passage includes chains from Zayd ibn Thabit, 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba, and Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab.
2
Evaluate the chain differences and their impact on authenticity
The author notes 'severe differences' that weaken the hadith. Identify the main problems: mursal vs. marfu', and the fact that senior students of al-Zuhri did not narrate it.
Weakness in sanad means the hadith is zanni al-thubut (speculative transmission).
3
Identify the textual variants (matn) and the preferred version
Al-Daraqutni reports two matn versions: one sa' and half a sa'. He declares the mursal version (half sa' from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab) as 'asahh' (more correct).
This matn difference directly affects the legal ruling.
4
Understand the juristic impact: two opinions on sadaqat al-fitr
The passage introduces the first opinion: half a sa' of wheat suffices, attributed to many Companions. The author implies a second opinion (full sa') will follow. Connect this to the Hanafi vs. Shafi'i dispute.
The Hanafi school adopts the first opinion; the Shafi'i school requires a full sa'.
5
Recognize the role of companion practice (athar) when hadith is weak
Ibn al-Mundhir says no reliable hadith exists; the Companions' practice was based on their own reasoning (ijtihad) considering scarcity and equivalence. This is istihsan (juristic preference).
This shows how weak dalil can be overridden by companion consensus and istihsan.

الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن قبيصة بن ذؤيب (١)، عن زيد بن ثابت. وأخرجه عبد الرزاق، والبخاري، والدارقطني، من طريق ابن جريج، عن الزهري، عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. وأخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، من طريق سفيان بن حسين، والبخاري، من طريق إبراهيم بن سعد الزهري، والطحاوي، والبيهقي كلاهما من طريق عبد الرحمن بن خالد وعقيل. أربعتهم: سفيان وإبراهيم وعبد الرحمن وعقيل عن الزهري، عن سعيد بن المسيب، به مرسلاً. هذا ما استطعت جمعه من طرق الحديث، وهذه الاختلافات الشديدة مضعفة للحديث للإشعار بعدم ضبط راويه. والحديث لم يقتصر على الخلاف في سنده، بل اختلف في متنه، قال الدارقطني: واختلفوا أيضاً في متنه في حديث سفيان بن حسين عن الزهري صاعاً من القمح وكذلك قال النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير، عن أبيه صاع من قمح عن كل إنسان، وفي حديث الآخرين نصف صاع قمح، وأصحهما عن الزهري، عن سعيد بن المسيب مرسلاً (١٠). قال ابن المنذر: لا نعلم في القمح خبراً ثابتاً عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يعتمد عليه، ولم يكن البر بالمدينة ذلك الوقت إلا الشيء اليسير منه، فلما كثر في زمن الصحابة رأوا أن منه يقوم مقام صاع من شعير وهم نصف صاع. (١١) وقال البيهقي: «وقد وردت أخبار عن النبي الله في صاع من بر، ووردت أخبار في نصف صاع ولا يصح شيء من ذلك، وقد بينت علة كل واحد منهما في الخلافيات» (١). وقال ابن عبد البر: «هذا نص في موضع الخلاف، إلا أنه لم يروه كبار أصحاب ابن شهاب، ولا من يحتج بروايته منهم إذا انفرد» (٢). أثر هذا الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء لهذا الحديث أثر في الفقه الإسلامي، إذ بنيت على هذا الحديث مسألتان فقهيتان، وترتب في ضوء العمل بهذا الحديث، وعدم العمل به خلاف فقهي بين أهل العلم. وسأسوق كل مسألة مفردة عن أختها. قولين: المسألة الأولى: إجزاء نصف صاع من البر في صدقة الفطر اختلف الفقهاء في إمكان إجزاء نصف صاع من البر في صدقة الفطر على القول الأول: يجزئ نصف صاع من البر لصدقة الفطر وهذا مروي عن أبي بكر الصديق (٣)، وعمر بن الخطاب (٤)، وعثمان بن عفان (٤)، وأسماء بنت أبي بكر (٧)، وعبد الله بن مسعود (٨)، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان (٩)
Chapter Three / Difference in Chain (Sanad) and Text (Matn). Qubaysa ibn Dhu'ayb (1) from Zayd ibn Thabit. And 'Abd al-Razzaq, al-Bukhari, and al-Daraqutni narrated it via Ibn Jurayj from al-Zuhri from 'Abdullah ibn Tha'laba from the Prophet (peace be upon him). And Ibn Abi Shayba narrated it via Sufyan ibn Husayn, and al-Bukhari via Ibrahim ibn Sa'd al-Zuhri, and al-Tahawi and al-Bayhaqi both via 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Khalid and 'Uqayl. All four: Sufyan, Ibrahim, 'Abd al-Rahman, and 'Uqayl from al-Zuhri from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, as a mursal report. This is what I could compile of the chains of the hadith, and these severe differences weaken the hadith, indicating that its narrator was not precise. The disagreement was not limited to its chain; they also differed in its text. Al-Daraqutni said: 'They also differed in its text in the hadith of Sufyan ibn Husayn from al-Zuhri: one sa' of wheat, and likewise al-Nu'man ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri from Tha'laba ibn Abi Sa'ir from his father: one sa' of wheat per person, and in the hadith of the others: half a sa' of wheat. The more correct of the two is from al-Zuhri from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab as a mursal (10).' Ibn al-Mundhir said: 'We do not know of a reliable report from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about wheat that can be relied upon. Wheat was scarce in Medina at that time; when it became abundant in the time of the Companions, they considered that half a sa' of it is equivalent to a sa' of barley (11).' And al-Bayhaqi said: 'Reports have come from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about one sa' of wheat, and reports about half a sa', but none of that is authentic, and I have clarified the defect of each in al-Khilafiyyat (1).' And Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: 'This is a text on the point of disagreement, except that the senior students of Ibn Shihab did not narrate it, nor those whose narration is accepted when they are solitary (2).' The impact of this hadith on juristic differences: This hadith had an impact on Islamic jurisprudence, for two legal issues are built upon it, and in light of acting upon this hadith or not acting upon it, a juristic difference occurred among scholars. I will present each issue separately. Two opinions: The first issue: The sufficiency of half a sa' of wheat for sadaqat al-fitr. The jurists differed on whether half a sa' of wheat suffices for sadaqat al-fitr. The first opinion: Half a sa' of wheat suffices for sadaqat al-fitr. This is related from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (3), 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (4), 'Uthman ibn 'Affan (4), Asma bint Abi Bakr (7), 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (8), and Mu'awiya ibn Abi Sufyan (9).

Commentary
This passage from the book 'The Effect of Differences in Chains and Texts on Juristic Differences' is a textual analysis of a specific hadith concerning the amount of wheat due for zakat al-fitr. The author first collects the various chains (turuq) of the hadith, noting multiple narrators and routes, all traced back to al-Zuhri. He observes that the differences in the chains, especially between mursal and muttasil, weaken the hadith, indicating a lack of precision in its transmission. Then he highlights differences in the text (matn): some versions mention one sa' of wheat, others half a sa'. Al-Daraqutni declares the mursal version (from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab) as the more correct. Ibn al-Mundhir and al-Bayhaqi further argue that no authentic report exists on this matter, and that the practice of the Companions, given the scarcity of wheat in early Medina, was to consider half a sa' of wheat equivalent to a full sa' of barley. Ibn 'Abd al-Barr adds that the key narrators of Ibn Shihab (al-Zuhri) did not transmit this hadith, affecting its reliability. The author then introduces the impact on fiqh: two issues arise from this hadith. Here he presents the first issue—whether half a sa' of wheat suffices for sadaqat al-fitr. He lists the first opinion (half a sa' suffices) and attributes it to several prominent Companions. This sets the stage for the second opinion (likely that it does not suffice) later. The passage demonstrates how weakness in sanad and matn leads to valid ikhtilaf among jurists.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (sadaqat al-fitr): The obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan, traditionally one sa' of staple food per person.
صاع (sa'): A unit of volume measure used for zakat al-fitr, approximately 2.5 kg of wheat.
بر (burr): Wheat, the grain in question for substitution in zakat al-fitr.
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.
متن (matn): The text or content of a hadith.

Impact of Hadith on Sadaqat al-Fitr: The Half Sa' Opinion
Presents first opinion on half sa' of wheat for sadaqat al-fitr and its supporters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first opinion regarding half a sa' of wheat for sadaqat al-fitr and explain how differences in hadith transmission led to its adoption by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Half a sa' of wheat for sadaqat al-fitr is a Hanafi view supported by Companions, despite hadith authenticity questions.

In this lesson, we explore how differences in hadith chains and texts (ikhtilaf fi al-sanad wa al-matn) generate juristic divergence. The specific issue is whether half a sa' of wheat suffices for sadaqat al-fitr. The passage presents the first opinion – that it does suffice – supported by a formidable list of Companions and early scholars, including Abu Hanifa. Understanding this opinion helps us grasp the Hanafi approach to accepting sound practice even when isolated hadith reports are disputed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two opinions
The passage states that the jurists differed on the sufficiency of half a sa' of wheat. Two opinions exist; the first is that it suffices. Note that this is a fiqh issue rooted in hadith analysis.
2
Recognize the dalil (reports)
The basis for the first opinion is a set of reports (akhbar) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about half a sa' of wheat for sadaqat al-fitr. These reports are ahad and their authenticity is questioned by al-Bayhaqi and Ibn Abd al-Barr.
The text quotes their criticisms: al-Bayhaqi says none are authentic; Ibn Abd al-Barr notes that major students of Ibn Shihab did not narrate them.
3
Note the criticisms of the reports
Al-Bayhaqi's and Ibn Abd al-Barr's statements cast doubt on the thubut (certainty of transmission) of the half sa' reports. This illustrates how sanad criticism impacts fiqh positions.
4
List the supporters of the first opinion
The passage lists numerous Companions (e.g., Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Asma, Ibn Masud), Successors (e.g., Mujahid, Urwa, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab), and early jurists (e.g., al-Hakam, Hammad, and notably Abu Hanifa). This demonstrates the weight of authority behind the position.
Some names appear twice (e.g., al-Hasan al-Basri) due to OCR, but the point is the extensive support.
5
Understand the thamarah (practical effect)
The practical consequence is that a Hanafi who gives half a sa' of wheat fulfills his obligation, whereas a Shafi'i would not. This is a concrete example of how differences in hadith acceptance lead to different legal obligations.

Al-Bayhaqi said: 'There are reports from the Prophet about one sa' of wheat, and reports about half a sa', but none of that is authentic, and I have explained the defect of each in al-Khilafiyyat.' Ibn Abd al-Barr said: 'This is a clear text in the point of difference, except that the major students of Ibn Shihab did not narrate it, nor those whose narration is accepted when solitary.' The effect of this hadith on the difference of jurists. This hadith has an impact on Islamic fiqh, as two legal issues are built upon it, and based on acting by this hadith or not, a juristic difference among scholars arises. I will present each issue separately. The First Issue: The sufficiency of half a sa' of wheat for Sadaqat al-Fitr. This passage is from a work analyzing how differences in hadith transmission and wording lead to juristic disagreements. Dispute over the Amount of Sadaqat al-Fitr for Wheat
Listing supporters of two opinions and their hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how differences in hadith chains and texts (ikhtilaf al-sanad wa al-mutn) lead to divergent rulings, and evaluate the use of hadith evidence by the Hanafi school versus the majority in the case of Sadaqat al-Fitr.

Core Idea
A single measurement dispute (half sa' vs full sa') reveals deep methodological splits in hadith authentication.

This passage presents a clear example of inter-madhhab disagreement rooted in differences of hadith evaluation. The Hanafi school, following companions like Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr, allows half a sa' of wheat for Sadaqat al-Fitr. The majority (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) insist on a full sa' for all food grains. The dispute hinges on which hadith is accepted and how its chain and text are interpreted. By working through this source, you will see how usul al-fiqh's tools of hadith criticism directly shape fiqh outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Read the passage and extract the two positions on the amount of wheat for Sadaqat al-Fitr. The first opinion (attributed to Abu Hanifa) allows a lesser amount (half sa'); the second requires a full sa' for all staples, including wheat.
The first opinion's precise amount is implied by the context of the dispute; the passage does not explicitly state 'half sa' for wheat' but that is the known Hanafi position.
2
List the supporters of each opinion
Note the long list of names for the first opinion (including companions and tabi'un) and the second opinion (including Aisha, Ibn Umar, and later imams). Observe that some figures (Ali, Ibn Abbas, Ibn al-Zubayr, Hasan al-Basri) appear in both lists, indicating variant narrations from them.
This shows that even within a single companion, multiple transmitted opinions can exist.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The first opinion relies on a previously mentioned hadith (al-hadith al-sabiq), not quoted here. The second group adduces the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri via Zayd ibn Aslam, which states that the Prophet's companions gave one sa' of various foods. They also reject the first group's hadith due to chain problems.
Notice the crucial role of chain evaluation: the second group dismisses the first group's proof on grounds of 'ikhtilaf kabir' (great disagreement) in its chain.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference in how each school evaluates the hadith evidence produces a concrete difference in obligation: Hanafi: half sa' of wheat is obligatory; other schools: one full sa' is obligatory.
This directly impacts millions of Muslims each year at the end of Ramadan.

والحجة لهم الحديث السابق الذكر والتفصيل.

القول الثاني : وهو أنه لا يجزئ في صدقة الفطر إلا صاع سواء كان من البر أو غيره، وهو المروي عن: عائشة، وعبد الله بن عمر، ومسروق، ومحمد بن سيرين، وأبي العالية، وغيرهم. الرواية الثانية عن: علي بن أبي طالب، وعبد الله بن عباس، وعبد الله بن الزبير، والحسن البصري. وذهب إلى هذا الإمام مالك، والشافعي، وأحمد بن حنبل.

Chapter Three: Difference in isnad and matn. Sufyan, al-Hakam, Hammad, Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim, Sa'd ibn Ibrahim, Ata, Mujahid, Urwa ibn al-Zubayr, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, Tawus, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, Abu Salama ibn Abd al-Rahman, Abd Allah ibn Shaddad, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, and others. The second narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib, Abd Allah ibn Abbas, Abd Allah ibn al-Zubayr, and al-Hasan al-Basri. And Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal went to this. And they did not use the hadith of Ibn Abi Sa'ir due to the great disagreement that occurred in it, and they used for their madhhab what was narrated by Zayd ibn Aslam, from Iyad ibn Abd Allah, from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, who said: We used to give Zakat al-Fitr - when the Messenger of Allah was among us - a sa' of food, or a sa' of barley, or a sa' of dates, or a sa' of raisins, or a sa' of dried yogurt. We continued giving it until Muawiya came to Medina and spoke. Among what he said to the people: 'I think that two mudds of Syrian wheat are equivalent to a sa' of this.'

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute analyzing how differences in hadith chains and texts lead to legal disagreements. The author first lists numerous companions and early scholars holding the first opinion, which is that half a sa' of wheat suffices for Sadaqat al-Fitr. This is attributed to Abu Hanifa as one of two narrations from Ali, Ibn Abbas, etc. The second opinion, held by Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad, and others, insists on a full sa' from any type including wheat. The Hanafi position relies on a different hadith (mentioned earlier). This illustrates how varying authenticity assessments and textual interpretations cause fiqh divergence.

Obligation of Zakat al-Fitr: A Hanafi-Maliki Dispute
Comparing views on poor's obligation for Zakat al-Fitr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Maliki positions on who must pay Zakat al-Fitr and identify the hadith principle that underlies the Hanafi view.

Core Idea
Zakat al-Fitr is not obligatory on the poor without surplus wealth according to Abu Hanifa, based on the principle 'No charity except from surplus wealth.'

This lesson examines a classical usul dispute between Abu Hanifa and Malik regarding the obligation of Zakat al-Fitr on the poor. The divergence arises from different interpretations of a key hadith and the weight given to various narrations. Understanding this disagreement will sharpen your ability to see how usul principles operate in fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Recognize that the issue is whether Zakat al-Fitr is wajib on the poor or only on the wealthy.
2
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
Abu Hanifa restricts obligation to the 'mutamakkin' (owner of 200 dirhams nisab). His proof is the hadith 'La sadaqata illa an zahr ghina'.
3
Examine the Maliki position(s)
Multiple narrations from Malik: some obligate if enough for 15 days or a month; some consider it 'zakat al-abdan'; some exempt those without means.
4
Analyze the usul principle
The key is how the hadith is interpreted. Hanafi: 'zahr ghina' means surplus wealth, thus poor not obligated. Maliki: various interpretations.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi exempts the poor; Maliki in some views obligates them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية: إيجاب صدقة الفطر على الفقير والغني اختلف الفقهاء في بيان ما إذا تجب زكاة الفطر على الفقير أم لا؟ فقد ذهب أبو حنيفة إلى أن زكاة الفطر تفرض على المتمكن فقط، ومعيار معرفة المتمكن لديه، هو أن يملك مئتي درهم". وحجته قوله لا صدقة إلا عن ظهر غنى»(۳). أما الإمام مالك فقد نقلت عنه عدة روايات منها : أن زكاة الفطر واجبة على الذي له معيشة خمسة عشر يوماً ونحوه أو شهراً ونحوه، وفي رواية قال: «إنما هي زكاة الأبدان»، وفي رواية أخرى: «إنها لا تجب على من ليس عنده».
The second issue: Making Zakat al-Fitr obligatory upon the poor and the rich. The jurists differed regarding whether Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon the poor or not. Abu Hanifa held that Zakat al-Fitr is only obligatory upon the capable person, and the criterion for capability according to him is that he owns two hundred dirhams. His proof is his statement: 'There is no charity except from surplus wealth.' As for Imam Malik, several narrations are transmitted from him, including that Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon one who has livelihood for fifteen days or so, or a month or so; in one narration he said: 'It is the charity of the bodies'; in another narration: 'It is not obligatory upon one who does not have it.'
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents a second fiqh dispute illustrating how differences in hadith and textual interpretation lead to varied rulings. The issue is whether a poor person is obligated to pay Zakat al-Fitr. Abu Hanifa restricts obligation to those who are 'capable' (mutamakkin), defined as owning 200 dirhams (nisab) beyond basic needs. His evidence is the hadith 'No charity except from surplus wealth', implying that one must have excess wealth to give. Imam Malik has multiple reported positions: some requiring a livelihood for 15 days or a month, some considering it a 'charity of the bodies' (i.e., mandatory on every individual regardless of wealth), and some exempting those without means. This shows how different scholars weigh the texts and principles. The Hanafi position prioritizes the general principle of affordability, while Malik's views reflect different interpretations of the same sources.

Key Terms
متمكن (mutamakkin): One who has sufficient wealth (200 dirhams) to be obligated to pay Zakat al-Fitr.
ظهر غنى (zahr ghina): Surplus wealth beyond immediate needs.
صدقة الفطر (Sadaqat al-Fitr): Zakat al-Fitr.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts Zakat al-Fitr obligation to those possessing nisab (200 dirhams) based on the hadith restricting charity to surplus wealth, while Malik's multiple views show the impact of variant narrations on the ruling.

Takhsis of General Command: The Hanafi Exemption of the Poor from Zakat al-Fitr
Madhhab dispute on obligation of Zakat al-Fitr for the poor, focusing on proof texts and usul principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of takhsis (restriction) to a general command and derive the ruling for a specific case (Zakat al-Fitr for the poor).

Core Idea
A general command can be restricted by a specific proof (takhsis al-amm); the poor are exempt from Zakat al-Fitr.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: does a general command to pay Zakat al-Fitr include the poor? The Hanafi school argues no, using the principle of takhsis (restriction) based on another hadith. Understanding how they derive this ruling will sharpen your ability to identify when a command is absolute and when it is qualified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing proofs
Locate the two hadiths cited: the general command 'Addu sadaqata al-fitr' (opposing side) and the specific hadith 'La sadaqata illa an zahri ghina' (Hanafi proof).
The first is general (amm), the second contains an exception (istithna).
2
Classify each text in Hanafi usul
Determine the textual category of each: the command 'Addu' is amm, while 'La sadaqata illa...' is khass due to the exclusive structure (لا...إلا).
Hanafis hold that amm is qat'i until a specific takhsis is proven.
3
Apply the rule of takhsis
Explain how the khass text restricts the amm: the exception ('except from surplus wealth') limits the obligation to those with surplus.
This is takhsis bi-l-mutafarriq (restriction by a separate text).
4
Derive the ruling for the poor
Conclude that a poor person without 200 dirhams (Hanafi nisab) is not obligated to pay Zakat al-Fitr.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
5
Compare with the opposing usul
The opposing school treats the command as general and does not accept the restricting hadith as sufficient takhsis, leading to obligation on the poor.
The dispute is over the authority and interpretation of the proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثَّالِث الاختلاف في السند والمتن
عمر: «أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فرض زكاة الفطر من رمضان على الناس صاعاً من تمر، أو صاعاً من شعير، على كل حر أو عبد ذكر أو أنثى، من المسلمين).

المسألة الثانية: إيجاب صدقة الفطر على الفقير والغني

اختلف الفقهاء في بيان ما إذا تجب زكاة الفطر على الفقير أم لا؟

فقد ذهب أبو حنيفة إلى أن زكاة الفطر تفرض على المتمكن فقط، ومعيار معرفة المتمكن لديه، هو أن يملك مئتي درهم".
وحجته قوله لا صدقة إلا عن ظهر غنى».

أما الإمام مالك فقد نقلت عنه عدة روايات منها : أن زكاة الفطر واجبة على الذي له معيشة خمسة عشر يوماً ونحوه أو شهراً ونحوه، وفي رواية قال: «إنما هي زكاة الأبدان»، وفي رواية أخرى: «إنها لا تجب على من ليس عنده»، وفي رواية الأخرى: «إنها واجبة على المحتاج أيضاً»، وفي رواية: «إن من له أخذ زكاة الفطر فهي تجب عليه، وفي رواية مشهورة عنه: «إن زكاة الفطر واجبة على الفقير الذي يفضل عن قوته وقوت من يمونه صاع كوجوبها على الغني»).

وذهب الشافعي إلى أنه من كان عنده فضل عن قوته وقوت من يمونه، وما يوفي به زكاته أداها عنه وعنهم، وإن لم يَكُنْ عنده إلا ما يؤدي عن بعضهم أداها عن بعضهم، وإن لم يكن عنده سوى مؤونته ومؤونتهم في يومه فليس عليه ولا على من يمونه زكاة.

وذهب إلى ذلك علي وأبو هريرة، وعطاء، وابن سيرين، وأبو سليمان، وهي إحدى الروايات عن مالك كما تقدم.

وذهب الإمام أحمد إلى أن زكاة الفطر واجبة على كل من تلزمه مؤونة نفسه إذا فضل عنده عن قوته وقوت عياله يوم قوته، وقوت عياله يوم العيد، وليلته صاعاً من أي صنف تجوز الزكاة منه، فإن لم يفضل عنده إلا أقل من صاع فيؤديه في إحدى الروايتين عنه، وفي الرواية الأخرى لا تجب عليه زكاة الفطر.

وذهب عبيد الله بن الحسن إلى أن من أصاب فضلاً عن غدائه وعشائه فعليه أن يأخذ ويعطي صدقة الفطر. وهنا يأتي دور حديث ابن أبي صعير السابق الذكر والتفصيل؛ فَهُوَ حجة لِمَنْ أوجب الصدقة عَلَى الفقير ؛ قال ابن قدامة: ولنا ما روي عن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير، عن أبيه؛ أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «أدوا صدقة الفطر ...»
Chapter Three: Disagreement in Chain and Text
Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, made obligatory Zakat al-Fitr from Ramadan upon the people: a sa' of dates or a sa' of barley upon every free person or slave, male or female, from the Muslims.'

The Second Issue: Making Sadaqat al-Fitr Obligatory on the Poor and the Rich

The jurists differed in clarifying whether Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon the poor or not.

Abu Hanifa held that Zakat al-Fitr is imposed only on the capable person, and his criterion for determining the capable person is that he possesses two hundred dirhams. His proof is his saying: 'No charity except from surplus wealth.'

As for Imam Malik, several narrations are reported from him: that Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon one who has a livelihood of fifteen days or so, or a month or so; and in one narration he said: 'It is zakat of bodies'; in another narration: 'It is not obligatory upon one who does not have [it]'; in another narration: 'It is obligatory upon the needy as well'; in another narration: 'One who receives Zakat al-Fitr, it is obligatory upon him'; and in a famous narration from him: 'Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon the poor person who has a surplus beyond his own food and the food of his dependents equal to a sa', just as it is obligatory upon the rich.'

Al-Shafi'i held that whoever has a surplus beyond his own food and the food of his dependents, and what he pays his zakat with, pays it for himself and for them; and if he only has enough to pay for some of them, he pays for some of them; and if he has only his own maintenance and theirs for the day, then there is no zakat upon him nor upon his dependents.

Ali, Abu Hurairah, 'Ata', Ibn Sirin, and Abu Sulaiman held that view, and it is one of the narrations from Malik as mentioned earlier.

Ahmad held that Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon everyone who is obliged to support himself if he has a surplus beyond his own food and the food of his family on the day of his food, and the food of his family on the day of 'Eid and its night, equal to a sa' of any type from which zakat is permissible; if he only has less than a sa', he pays it according to one of two narrations from him, and in the other narration it is not obligatory upon him to pay Zakat al-Fitr.

'Ubayd Allah ibn al-Hasan held that whoever obtains a surplus beyond his lunch and dinner must take and give Sadaqat al-Fitr. This is where the role of the previously mentioned and detailed hadith of Ibn Abi Sa'ir comes in; it is an argument for those who make sadaqah obligatory upon the poor. Ibn Qudamah said: We have that which is narrated from Tha'laba ibn Abi Sa'ir, from his father, that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Pay Sadaqat al-Fitr...'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the obligation of Zakat al-Fitr on the poor. The author presents the Hanafi position first: it is only obligatory on the one who is 'mutamakkin' (capable), defined as possessing 200 dirhams (nisab). Abu Hanifa's proof is the hadith 'La sadaqata illa an zahri ghina' (no charity except from surplus wealth). Then Imam Malik's various transmitted positions are listed, including the famous one that it is obligatory on the poor if they have a surplus of one sa' after their own and dependents' food. Al-Shafi'i and others require a surplus beyond the day's maintenance. Ahmad has two narrations, one requiring payment even if less than a sa', the other waiving it. The passage concludes by citing the hadith of Tha'laba ibn Abi Sa'ir as evidence for those who obligate it on the poor, as argued by Ibn Qudamah. The discussion demonstrates how differing interpretations of proof texts lead to divergent fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
زكاة الفطر (zakāt al-fiṭr): A required charity given at the end of Ramadan, usually one sa' of staple food per person.
المتمكن (al-mutamakkin): A person who possesses nisab (200 dirhams) and is therefore considered capable of paying zakat.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): The usul principle of restricting a general text (amm) by a specific evidence.
استثناء (istithnāʾ): An exception, usually expressed by 'illa' (except), which creates a khass text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi criterion for being obligated to pay Zakat al-Fitr?
A) Having any surplus food for one day
B) Possessing 200 dirhams (nisab)
C) Being an adult Muslim
D) Being the head of a household

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi exemption of the poor from Zakat al-Fitr illustrates the usul principle of takhsis: a general command can be restricted by a specific proof, and the exclusive structure 'except' creates a decisive restriction.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
How Hadith Wording Variations Generate Fiqh Differences in Marriage
Listing variant hadith narrations on marriage wording and concluding their impact on juristic differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how variations in hadith wording (riwayat bi al-ma'na) generate legitimate differences among fuqaha in marriage expressions, and apply the usul principle that narration by meaning is permissible when the meaning is preserved.

Core Idea
Variant hadith wordings do not weaken authenticity; they generate legitimate fiqh ikhtilaf on marriage expressions.

In this lesson, we examine a hadith narrated through multiple chains with slightly different wordings for the marriage contract. You will see how scholars like Ibn Hajar explain that these variations do not harm authenticity but rather reflect narration by meaning. This textual variety directly impacted the rulings of the schools: all agree on two expressions (تزويج and إنكاح) but differ on others such as ملكت. By understanding this, you will grasp a key usul principle: legitimate ikhtilaf can arise from a single sound hadith when transmitted with synonymous wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its variants
List the different marriage expressions found in the multiple chains: ملكتكها, زوجتكها, أنكحتكها, أملكناكها, etc. Note each chain narrator.
The core hadith is about the man who married with an iron ring.
2
Analyze the authenticity despite variations
Read Ibn Hajar's comment: the variations do not weaken the hadith; they indicate narration by meaning (riwayat bi al-ma'na). The Prophet ﷺ said one phrase, and narrators conveyed it with synonyms.
Most narrations are in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Connect variations to fiqh ikhtilaf
Trace how each wording could be taken as a permissible expression for marriage. Some scholars restrict to the exact words used, others accept any clear synonym.
The variation creates scope for different interpretations.
4
State the unanimous and disputed positions
All scholars agree that تزويج or إنكاح are valid. They differ on other expressions like ملكت. The passage lists that there are ‘madhahib’ without detailing them here.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
5
Extract the usul lesson
Riwayat bi al-ma'na is permissible in the Hanafi school (and others) as long as the meaning is preserved. This principle broadens the range of acceptable marriage formulas.
It also shows that ikhtilaf is not always due to weak proofs.

Note: In Ahmad edition Ihya' al-Turath al-'Arabi (6/457-458) it has 'I married her', and in Mu'assasat al-Risala (37/487) it has 'I give her to you in marriage', and similarly in al-Afkar al-Dawliyya (4/1694). Narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq from Ma'mar from Abi Hazim. And al-Tabarani in al-Kabir (5750) from al-Layth from Hisham ibn Sa'd from Abi Hazim, in it: 'I marry her to you'. And al-Tabarani in al-Kabir (5938) via Muhammad ibn Aban from Mubashshir ibn Maksar from Abi Hazim, in it: 'I have married you'. And Muslim (4/144, 1425, 77) from 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Muhammad al-Darawurdi from Abi Hazim, in it: 'I give her to you in marriage'. Despite this, this difference did not detract in the view of scholars. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: 'Most of these narrations are in the two Sahihs. It is extremely far-fetched that Sahl ibn Sa'd witnessed this story from beginning to end many times, hearing each time a wording different from what he heard in the other story. Rather, this may be known with certainty as well. What is certain is that the Prophet ﷺ did not say all these wordings in one single moment. So nothing remains except to say that the Prophet ﷺ said one of them, and the rest of the narrators conveyed by meaning.' The Impact of This Hadith on Juristic Differences. Due to the variation and multiplicity of these narrations, there is a clear impact on Islamic fiqh, for juristic differences were built upon these narrations concerning what expressions of marriage render the marriage contract valid, as follows: The scholars agreed that marriage is concluded with the word تزويج or إنكاح, and they differed on the validity of marriage with other expressions according to schools, which are:
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an extended textual analysis showing how variant wordings in the hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd (the marriage of a man with an iron ring) gave rise to juristic differences. The author first exhaustively lists multiple chains of narration, each with slightly different wording: ملكتكها (I give her to you in marriage), زوجتكها (I marry her to you), أنكحتكها (I give her to marriage), أملكناكها (we give her to you in marriage), etc. He then quotes Ibn Hajar to explain that this variation does not undermine the hadith's authenticity; narrators transmitted the meaning, not the exact words. The core of the lesson is that this textual variation directly impacted fiqh: all scholars agree that marriage is valid with the words تزويج (to marry) and إنكاح (to give in marriage), but they differ on whether other words like ملكت (I give) are sufficient. The passage concludes by indicating that there are multiple schools on this issue, though it does not detail them here. This illustrates a key usul principle: differences in hadith wording can lead to legitimate differences in fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwij): Marriage; the verbal noun meaning 'to marry'.
إنكاح (inkāḥ): Marriage; the verbal noun meaning 'to give in marriage'.
ملكت (malaktu): I give (her to you) in marriage; from the root m-l-k, used as a marriage expression.
الرواية بالمعنى (al-riwayah bi al-ma'na): Narrating a hadith by meaning rather than exact wording; permissible when meaning is preserved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On which two expressions for marriage did all scholars agree?
A) تزويج and إنكاح
B) ملكت and أنكحت
C) زوجت and ملكت
D) أنكحت and زوجت

Key Takeaway
The variation in hadith wording does not weaken authenticity but rather generates legitimate ikhtilaf among scholars, illustrating the usul principle of narration by meaning.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
15 min
How Hadith Word Variants Create Fiqh Differences: The Marriage Contract Words Case
Usul case study: effect of hadith variants on fiqh dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of riwaya bi al-ma'na (narration by meaning) to explain why Abu Hanifa permits marriage with any tamlik word, and evaluate the Zahiri counter-argument.

Core Idea
Hadith narrations by meaning generate fiqh flexibility; literal acceptance of all variants creates restriction.

Imagine a single hadith reported with ten different wordings for the marriage contract. Did the Prophet say all of them at once, or did narrators paraphrase? This question is not just about history—it directly affects fiqh: which words actually conclude a valid marriage? In this lesson, we will examine the famous hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd and see how its variants split the schools. The Hanafis, following Abu Hanifa, see the variants as permission for any wording indicating transfer of ownership. The Zahiris see them as a precise list of permissible expressions. You will learn how the usul principle of riwaya bi al-ma'na (transmission by meaning) shapes this core fiqh difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its variants
First, locate the hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd and list the different wordings cited in the text: 'malkatuha', 'amalkatukaha', 'zawwajtukaha', and others.
These are all from the same event according to Ibn Hajar.
2
Note the two positions
Identify the Hanafi position (any tamlik word suffices) and the Zahiri position (only explicit words like tazwij or inkah).
The second school includes al-Thawri, al-Hasan ibn Salih, Abu Thawr, Abu 'Ubayd, and Abu Hanifa.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi school argues that the existence of multiple wordings proves that the meaning—transfer of ownership—is sufficient. The Prophet said one wording; narrators conveyed the sense.
This relies on the principle of riwaya bi al-ma'na, accepted by most Hanafis.
4
Examine the Zahiri dalil
Dawud al-Zahiri and Ibn Hazm argue that all variants are authentic and the Prophet taught each one separately to prescribe a limited set of valid expressions.
They cite a hadith that the Prophet repeated words three times for clarity.
5
Compare the usul principles
Compare the two approaches: riwaya bi al-ma'na (Hanafi) vs. literal adherence to all variants (Zahiri). Consider how each shapes the derived fiqh ruling.
This illustrates how usul al-fiqh directly influences furu'.

The Third Chapter: Difference in Isnad and Matn. And despite this, this difference did not detract in the view of scholars. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: 'Most of these narrations are in the Sahihain. It is very far-fetched that Sahl ibn Sa'd witnessed this event from beginning to end many times, and heard each time a wording different from what he heard in the other story. Rather, it may be known with certainty as well. It is certain that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not say all these wordings in one single hour. So nothing remains except to say that the Prophet (peace be upon him) spoke one wording, and the remaining narrators narrated it by meaning.' The effect of this hadith on the difference of jurists: The difference and multiplicity of these narrations has a prominent effect in Islamic fiqh. Based on these narrations, fiqh differences arose regarding which wording of marriage contract (tazwij) is valid. They are as follows: The scholars are unanimously agreed that marriage (nikah) is concluded with the wording 'tazwij' or 'inkah'. They differed regarding the conclusion of marriage with other than these wordings, according to schools: (76) From Qutayba ibn Sa'id, and in it: 'I have given her in ownership (malkatuha)'. ... [list of narrations]. The First School: Marriage cannot be concluded except with the word 'zawaj' or 'inkah', or 'tamlik' or 'imkan', and it is not valid with the word 'hiba'. This is the school of Dawud al-Zahiri and Ibn Hazm, arguing with the difference of narrations in the hadith. Ibn Hazm cited the different narrations then said: 'All of that is authentic.' Then he narrated through Bukhari from Anas ibn Malik from the Prophet: 'When he spoke a word, he would repeat it three times so that it would be understood from him.' Then he said: 'It is established that these are all wordings that he (peace be upon him) spoke, teaching us what marriage is concluded with.' The Second School: The validity of marriage with any wording indicating tamlik (making ownership). This is the school of al-Thawri, al-Hasan ibn Salih, Abu Thawr, Abu 'Ubayd, and Abu Hanifa. The first example: What Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated from Abu Mu'awiya al-Darir Muhammad ibn Khazim...
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a case study in how differences in hadith matn (text) cause differences in fiqh rulings. The author examines the famous hadith of Sahl ibn Sa'd regarding the marriage of a woman to a man who owned nothing but an iron ring. The hadith occurs in multiple narrations with different wordings for the contract: 'malkatuha', 'amalkatukaha', 'zawwajtukaha', etc. The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, holds that any wording that conveys tamlik (transfer of ownership) is valid for marriage, based on the principle that these different wordings were used by the Prophet (pbuh) or narrated by the Companions by meaning. The opposing school, represented by Dawud al-Zahiri and Ibn Hazm, argues that the Prophet repeated each wording to teach the precise permissible expressions, and therefore only those explicit words are valid. The passage thus illustrates the usul principle that when a hadith is transmitted with variant wordings, the fiqh ruling derived from it may vary depending on whether one sees the variants as equally authoritative or as a single event reported with permissible paraphrase.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwij): لفظ الزواج الصريح في عقد النكاح (the explicit word for marriage contract)
تمليك (tamlik): إعطاء الملكية (giving ownership), used metaphorically for marriage
إنكاح (inkah): لفظ النكاح الصريح (the explicit word for marriage)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The school of Dawud al-Zahiri and Ibn Hazm
B) The school of al-Thawri, al-Hasan ibn Salih, Abu Thawr, Abu 'Ubayd, and Abu Hanifa
C) The school of Imam al-Shafi'i
D) The school of Imam Malik

Key Takeaway
The same hadith, when transmitted with variant wordings, can support different fiqh rulings depending on whether one applies the principle of riwaya bi al-ma'na (Hanafi) or literal adherence (Zahiri).

Valid Wording for Marriage Contract: Hanafi vs Zahiri
Two madhhabs on permissible sigah for nikah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Zahiri positions on valid wording for a marriage contract and identify the usul principle (interpretation of hadith narrations) that underlies their disagreement.

Core Idea
A marriage contract is valid by any wording indicating tamlik, not just specific Quranic terms.

This passage compares two madhhabs on the permissible sigah (wording) for a marriage contract. The Zahiris restrict valid wording to specific terms (zawaj, inkah, tamlik, imkan) based on hadith narrations, while Hanafis allow any wording that clearly indicates tamlik (ownership transfer). Understanding this dispute reveals how different schools balance textual specificity against linguistic flexibility in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what wording Hanafis allow. The source says 'any wording indicating tamlik'.
Tamlik means transferring ownership or control of the marriage bond.
2
State the Zahiri position
Identify the restricted list: zawaj, inkah, tamlik, imkan. Hibah is excluded.
Ibn Hazm and Dawood al-Zahiri hold this view.
3
Analyze the Zahiri proof
They cite different hadith narrations where the Prophet used various wordings and repeated words three times, arguing that these specific terms are what he taught for the contract.
The hadith from Bukhari is used to support the idea that only those exact wordings are valid.
4
Identify the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that the hadith shows permissible wordings but does not restrict to those alone; the key is the meaning (tamlik), not the form.
They interpret the hadith as exemplifying, not enumerating, valid terms.
5
Determine the usul root
This is a dispute over textual 'umum (generality) versus khushul (specificity). Zahiris treat the hadith as exhaustive; Hanafis as indicative.
The principle of 'al-ibra bi al-ma'ani la bi al-alfaz' (consideration of meanings, not words) applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الأول: لا يجوز عقد النكاح إلا بلفظ الزواج أو الإنكاح، أو التمليك أو الإمكان، ولا يجوز بلفظ الهبة، وهو مذهب داود الظاهري وابن حزم محتجين باختلاف الروايات الواردة في الحديث، وقد ساق ابن حزم الروايات المختلفة ثم قال: «كل ذلك صحيح)، ثم روى من طريق البخاري عن أنس بن مالك عن النَّبي : «إنه كان إذا تكلّم بالكلمة أعادها ثلاثاً حتى تفهم عنه (۲) ، ثم قال: «فصح أنها ألفاظ كلها قالها عليه الصلاة والسلام معلماً لنا ما ينعقد به النكاح». المذهب الثاني جواز عقد النكاح بأي لفظ دال على التمليك، وهو مذهب الثوري، والحسن بن صالح، وأبي ثور، وأبي عبيد، وأبي حنيفة).
First madhhab: The marriage contract is not valid except with the wording of marriage (zawaj) or giving in marriage (inkah), or ownership (tamlik) or possibility (imkan), and it is not valid with the wording of gift (hibah). This is the madhhab of Dawood al-Zahiri and Ibn Hazm, arguing based on the differing narrations in the hadith. Ibn Hazm cited the different narrations and then said: 'All of that is authentic.' Then he narrated from al-Bukhari via Anas ibn Malik from the Prophet: 'When he spoke a word, he would repeat it three times so that it would be understood from him.' Then he said: 'So it is established that these are all wordings that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, teaching us what the marriage contract is concluded with.' Second madhhab: The permissibility of concluding the marriage contract with any wording indicating ownership (tamlik). This is the madhhab of al-Thawri, al-Hasan ibn Salih, Abu Thawr, Abu Ubayd, and Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between two madhhabs regarding the permissible wording for a marriage contract. The first madhhab, held by Dawood al-Zahiri and Ibn Hazm, restricts the valid wording to specific terms: zawaj, inkah, tamlik, and imkan, and excludes hibah. Their evidence comes from different narrations of a hadith where the Prophet repeated words three times, indicating that these specific words are what he taught. The second madhhab, which includes Imam Abu Hanifa and others, allows any wording that clearly indicates ownership (tamlik). The Hanafi position is one of flexibility, focusing on the meaning rather than specific forms. This dispute is rooted in how the different hadith narrations are interpreted. The author will further analyze the hadith in the next example.

Key Terms
لفظ النكاح (lafz al-nikah): The wording used to conclude a marriage contract.
التمليك (al-tamlik): The concept of transferring ownership or control.
الهبة (al-hibah): Gift; a transfer of ownership without consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Hanafis validate marriage contracts with any wording that clearly indicates tamlik, prioritizing meaning over specific linguistic forms, in contrast to Zahiri strict literal adherence.

Dispute on Obligatory Wiping in Tayammum: Evaluating a Hadith vs. Qiyas
Dispute: first opinion on tayammum wiping extent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a hadith that appears to conflict with qiyas and apply Hanafi usul principles to reconcile or prefer one over the other.

Core Idea
Ahad hadith may be interpreted or set aside when it contradicts qiyas based on a clear text.

This lesson examines the first opinion on the obligatory amount of wiping in tayammum: Imam al-Zuhri’s view that it extends to the armpits, based on the hadith of Ammar about wiping to the shoulders. Although the passage is truncated, we can analyze how the Hanafi school would respond using usul principles, contrasting literal interpretation with analogical reasoning from wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opinion and its evidence
State Imam al-Zuhri’s position: wiping the hands up to the armpits is obligatory. Identify his proof: the hadith of Ammar (wiped to the shoulders).
The hadith is quoted in the passage.
2
Classify the hadith (thubut and dalala)
Determine the transmission strength and clarity of the hadith. Is it mutawatir or ahad? Is the wording precise or open to interpretation?
The hadith is ahad and the term 'shoulders' may be ambiguous.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle on conflicting proofs
Recall that Hanafi usul gives priority to qiyas over ahad hadith when the hadith contradicts a stronger proof (e.g., Quranic ayat that are general or qiyas based on them).
The Quranic verse on tayammum mentions 'hands' generally; wudu's limit is elbows by clear text and consensus.
4
Derive the Hanafi position
Using qiyas: tayammum replaces wudu, so the same limit (elbows) applies. The hadith of Ammar is either interpreted figuratively or considered abrogated/weak.
The Hanafi position (not in the given text) is that wiping to the elbows is obligatory.
5
Evaluate the dispute's outcome
Compare the two positions and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference): one wipes to armpits, the other to elbows. The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
The dispute shows how usul principles shape fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية: المقدار الواجب مسحه في التيمم
اختلف الفُقَهَاء في المقدار الواجب مسحه في فرض التيمم على أقوال: القَوْل الأول: يَجِبُ مسح اليدين إلى الإبطين، وَهُوَ مَذْهَب الإمام الزُّهْرِيّ، وحجته: حَدِيث عَمَّار الأول السابق الذكر: «تيممنا مَع رَسُول الله فمسحنا بوجوهنا وأيدينا إلى المناكب».
The Second Issue: The obligatory amount to wipe in tayammum. The jurists differed regarding the obligatory amount to wipe in the obligatory tayammum into several opinions: The first opinion: It is obligatory to wipe the hands up to the armpits, and this is the madhhab of Imam az-Zuhri. His evidence is the hadith of Ammar mentioned earlier: 'We performed tayammum with the Messenger of Allah, and we wiped our faces and hands up to the shoulders.'
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the obligatory extent of wiping in tayammum. It records the difference among fuqaha. The first opinion mentioned is that of Imam az-Zuhri, who holds that the hands must be wiped up to the armpits. His proof is the hadith of Ammar ibn Yasir, which states that they wiped to the shoulders (including armpits). The passage is truncated; normally other opinions follow, such as the majority (including Hanafi) who say to the elbows, based on the analogy with wudu and other hadith. The Hanafi position is that tayammum replaces wudu, so the same limit (elbows) applies.

Key Terms
المناكب (al-manakib): Shoulders; in the hadith, indicating wiping up to the shoulder joint.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Quranic verse on tayammum.

C) Analogy with wudu.
D) Consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
A hadith ahad can be overridden by qiyas when the qiyas is based on stronger evidence, reflecting the Hanafi hierarchy of proofs.

Usul Analysis: How Chain Weakness and Qiyas Shape the Tayammum Wiping Dispute
Analyzing hadith and qiyas proofs for three positions on tayammum wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate how differences in hadith chain strength and the use of qiyas lead to divergent rulings on the extent of wiping in tayammum.

Core Idea
A weak hadith can still be used when supported by qiyas; a disconnected hadith loses its evidentiary force.

The extent of wiping in tayammum (dry ablution) is a classic problem illustrating how usul al-fiqh principles—especially the authentication of hadith and the role of qiyas—lead to ikhtilaf among madhahib. In this passage, you will see how Hanafis combine weak narrations with qiyas to derive wiping to the elbows, while Shafi'is rely on a Quranic verse and a disconnected hadith. A third group limits wiping to the wrists based on the literal meaning of 'hands' and an alternative narration. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to weigh conflicting evidences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three rulings on the extent of wiping: (1) to elbows (Hanafi, new Shafi'i), (2) to elbows via old Shafi'i also, (3) to wrists (old Shafi'i, Ahmad, Zaydis, Zahiris).
2
Examine the Hanafi evidence
Analyze the two hadiths cited by Hanafis (hadith of 'Ammar and hadith of Abu Umama) and note the criticism of their chains (weak narrators, fabricators). Then study the qiyas argument by al-Sarakhsi linking tayammum to wudu.
The qiyas is based on the principle that a substitute (badal) follows the original in its rulings.
3
Examine the Shafi'i evidence
The Shafi'is cite Quran 5:6 ('wa aydikum') and a hadith of Ibn al-Simmah. Note that the hadith is disconnected (munqati') because al-A'raj did not hear from Ibn al-Simmah.
A disconnected hadith is considered da'if and not a valid proof by itself.
4
Assess the third position's evidence
The third group relies on the minimal literal meaning of 'hands' (kaf) and a second narration of 'Ammar's hadith that mentions only palms.
This position avoids the weak chains but may neglect the qiyas analogy.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
Apply the usul principle: a weak hadith can be strengthened by multiple chains and by qiyas. The Hanafi position combines both, while the Shafi'i position relies on a disconnected hadith. The third position lacks strong support. Conclude why the Hanafi view is considered stronger.
Tarjih is based on the preponderance of evidence, not on a single rigid rule.

Chapter Three / Difference in Chain and Text
313
He struck his palms on the ground, then raised them to his face, then struck another strike and wiped his forearms, their inner and outer sides, until he reached the elbows with his hands. Recorded by al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi. al-Haythami said: 'In it is al-Rabi' ibn Badr, and they have unanimously agreed on his weakness.' al-Bayhaqi said: 'al-Rabi' ibn Badr is weak, but he is not unique in this.' Ibn Daqiq al-'Id responded to him, saying: 'al-Bayhaqi's statement that he is not unique is not sufficient for using it as evidence until his rank and the rank of his partner are examined, for not everyone who agrees with others in narration necessitates strength and acceptability.'
They also used as evidence the hadith of Abu Umama from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Tayammum is one strike for the face and one strike for the hands up to the elbows.' al-Haythami said: al-Tabarani narrated it in al-Kabir, and in it is Ja'far ibn al-Zubayr, about whom Shu'bah said: He fabricated four hundred hadiths.
They also used qiyas: al-Sarakhsi said: 'Tayammum is a substitute for wudu, and wudu (involves washing) the hands up to the elbows; so tayammum is likewise. Its explanation: In tayammum two limbs are omitted entirely, and two limbs remain, so tayammum on them is like wudu on all (limbs), just as prayer during travel has two rak'ahs omitted, and the remaining (rak'ahs) are in the state of completeness; for this reason we required full coverage (istighraq) in tayammum.'
... (footnotes omitted for brevity)
[PAGE_314]
314
Chapter Three / Difference in Chain and Text
As for the Shafi'is: They also hold that wiping (extends) to the elbows, and that the elbows are included in tayammum. They adduce as evidence the statement of Allah: 'and your hands from it' (5:6). They said: The absolute name 'hand' includes the shoulder, so the forearm is included in the generality of the name, then in tayammum it is restricted by analogy to wudu. al-Shafi'i narrated from the hadith of al-A'raj from Ibn al-Simmah: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) performed tayammum and wiped his face and his forearms.' But this hadith is defective due to disconnection because al-A'raj did not hear from Ibn al-Simmah. Abu Thawr and al-Za'farani transmitted from Imam al-Shafi'i in the old (qadim) that he said: up to the wrists. al-Nawawi rejected this transmission.
The third opinion: Wiping the hands up to the wrist. This is reported from 'Ali, and it is the school of Imam Ahmad, the Zaydis, and the Zahiris. Their evidence is that wiping the palms to the wrist is the minimum that falls under the name 'hands,' and they also used the second hadith of 'Ammar.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage discusses the juristic dispute regarding the extent of wiping in tayammum. The author presents three main positions: (1) wiping to the elbows (the Hanafi and Shafi'i position), (2) wiping to the wrist (the old Shafi'i view and the view of Ahmad, Zaydis, and Zahiris), and (3) wiping to the wrist (third position). The passage first presents the proofs of those who say to the elbows: a hadith via al-Rabi' ibn Badr (weak but not unique), a hadith via Abu Umama (containing a fabricator), and a qiyas by al-Sarakhsi that tayammum replaces wudu, so the extent of wiping is analogous to washing in wudu. Then it presents the Shafi'i proofs: the Quranic verse 'and your hands' and a hadith via al-A'raj (disconnected). Finally, it notes the third opinion with its proof. The author critically examines the hadith chains, showing how weakness and discontinuity affect the strength of evidence. This demonstrates the usul principle that the authenticity and continuity of a hadith (sanad) directly impact the ruling derived from it.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable
المرفق (al-mirfaq): The elbow joint, the end of the forearm
الكوع (al-ku'): The wrist joint

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Hanafi school accepts the hadith of 'Ammar (which mentions wiping to the elbows) despite its weak chain?
A) The hadith is saheeh according to al-Tabarani.
B) The weakness is compensated by qiyas to wudu and multiple narrations.
C) The hadith is mutawatir.
D) They follow a different definition of 'weak'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on tayammum wiping to the elbows illustrates how weak hadiths can be strengthened through qiyas and multiple narrations, a key usul principle that distinguishes their methodology.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
Four Views on Doubt in Prayer: Hadith Interpretation Drives Ikhtilaf
A survey of four scholarly positions on doubt in prayer, highlighting hadith-based reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four scholarly positions on doubt in rak'at, explain the Hanafi view's hadith evidence, and evaluate how differences in sanad and matn lead to juristic divergence.

Core Idea
Doubt in prayer triggers four rulings; the Hanafi view uniquely invalidates first-time doubt and uses predominant probability for habitual doubt.

This passage presents four distinct scholarly positions on how to resolve doubt (shakk) in the number of rak'at during prayer. It serves as a concrete example of the book's core thesis: that differences in hadith transmission (sanad) and texts (matn) directly cause juristic differences. The Hanafi position stands out by distinguishing between first-time and habitual doubt, relying on the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate each view and analyze the proof structure that underpins the Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Views
Extract from the passage the four positions: (1) Zahiri/early scholars – sajdat al-sahw without specification; (2) Ahmad – distinction between imam and ma'mum with dual narrations; (3) Abu Hanifah – distinction between first-time (invalid) and habitual (build on predominant probability); (4) Several Companions – build on certainty (lower number).
Note that the fourth view is attributed to Abu Bakr, 'Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, etc.
2
Locate the Hanafi Position and Its Key Condition
Focus on the Hanafi view: if doubt occurs for the first time, prayer is invalid (because certainty is required for worship). If doubt is habitual and recurrent, he must perform taharrī (exertion to reach predominant probability), and if no probability arises, he builds on the minimum.
This is a unique Hanafi distinction not found in other views.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Dalil: Hadith of Ibn 'Awf
The Hanafi proof is the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, which commands building on the lower number and prostrating before salam. Evaluate its chain (hasan sahih per Tirmidhi) and its explicit command structure.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its dalalah (meaning) is clear and specific.
4
Compare with Other Dalils
The other views rely on hadith from Abu Hurairah and Ibn Mas'ud, which also command sajdat al-sahw but do not specify building on a particular number. The Hanafi response is that Ibn 'Awf's hadith is more specific and directly addresses the procedure.
This illustrates how preference for one hadith over another can alter the ruling.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi – first-time doubt invalidates; other schools – prayer is valid with sajdat al-sahw. For habitual doubt, Hanafi requires taharrī; others build on certainty immediately.
Understanding this difference is crucial for fatwa and practice.

الأنصاري، وإبراهيم النخعي، والحسن البصري، وعطاء. وَهُوَ مَذْهَب الظاهرية). والحجة لَهُمْ:
.. الحَدِيث السابق.
.. وحديث أبِي هُرَيْرَة مرفوعاً: «إذا لم يدر أحدكم كم صلى ثلاثاً أو أربعاً فليسجد سجدتين وَهُوَ جالس»
. وحديث ابن مَسْعُود مرفوعاً: «إنما أنا بشر، فإذا نسيت فذكروني، إذا أوهم أحدكم في صلاته فليتحر أقرب ذلك من الصَّوَاب، ثُمَّ ليتم عَلَيْهِ، ثُمَّ يسجد سجدتين». القَوْل الثَّانِي: هُوَ قَوْل الإمام أحمد - التفصيل بَيْنَ الإمام والمأموم، وفي كليهما رِوَايَتَانِ فنقل الأثرم عَنْهُ أن الإمام يبني عَلَى غالب الظن، وفيه رِوَايَة أخرى البناء عَلَى اليقين، وَهِيَ الَّتِي صححها أبو الخطاب، أما إذا كَانَ منفرداً أو مأموماً فيبني عَلَى اليقين وفيه رِوَايَة أُخْرَى أَنَّهُ يبني عَلَى غلبة الظن).

۳۱۸
الفصل الثالث: الاختلاف في السند والمتن
القَوْل الثَّالِث: - وَهُوَ قَوْل أبي حَنِيْفَة - إن كَانَ شكه في ذلك مرة، بطلت صلاته، وإن كَانَ الشك يعتاده ويتكرر لَهُ، يبني عَلَى غالب الظن بحكم التحري، فإن لَمْ يقع لَهُ ظن بني على الأقل. والحجة لهذا المذهب ما روي من حَدِيث عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن عوف مرفوعاً: «إذا شك أحدكم في صلاته فلم يدر واحدةً صلى أو اثنتين فليبن عَلَى وَاحِدَة، فإن لَمْ يدر ثنتين صلى أو ثلاثاً فليبن عَلَى ثنيتين، فإن لَم يدر ثلاثاً صلى أو أربعاً فليين على ثلاث، وليسجد سجدتين قَبْلَ أَن يسلم»، قَالَ التِّرْمِذِي عَنْهُ: «هَذَا حَدِيث حَسَن صحيح». وَقَدْ شدد ابن حزم النكير عَلَى هَذَا القَوْل. القول الرابع: قالوا: من شك في صلاته فلم يدر أثلاثاً صلى أم أربعاً، فعليه أن يبني عَلَى مَا استيقن. وهذا القَوْل مروي عن أبي بكر وعمر، وابن مَسْعُود، وعلي بن أبي طالب، وابن عُمَر، وابن عَبَّاس، وبه قال سعيد بن جبير، وعطاء، والأوزاعي.
Chapter Three: Difference in Sanad and Matn. 317. Al-Ansari, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, and 'Ata'. And it is the madhhab of the Zahiriyya. Their evidence: The previous hadith. And the hadith of Abu Hurairah marfu'an: 'If one of you does not know whether he prayed three or four, let him prostrate two prostrations while sitting.' And the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud marfu'an: 'I am only a human, so if I forget, remind me. If one of you is uncertain in his prayer, let him try to determine the closest to what is correct, then complete upon it, then prostrate two prostrations.' The second view: It is the view of Imam Ahmad – detail between imam and ma'mum, and in both there are two narrations. Al-Athram narrated from him that the imam builds on predominant probability, and in another narration, building on certainty, and this is the one Abu al-Khattab deemed sahih. As for the one praying alone or the ma'mum, he builds on certainty, and there is another narration that he builds on predominant probability. 318. Chapter Three: Difference in Sanad and Matn. The third view: – and it is the view of Abu Hanifah – if his doubt occurs once, his prayer is invalid; but if doubt is habitual and recurrent for him, he builds on predominant probability by way of investigation (taharrī); if no probability occurs to him, he builds on the minimum. The evidence for this madhhab is what was narrated from the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf marfu'an: 'If one of you doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed one or two, let him build on one; if he does not know two or three, let him build on two; if he does not know three or four, let him build on three, and let him prostrate two prostrations before salam.' Al-Tirmidhi said about it: 'This is a hasan sahih hadith.' Ibn Hazm severely criticized this view. The fourth view: They said: Whoever doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed three or four, he must build on what he is certain of. This view is narrated from Abu Bakr, 'Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, Ibn 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas, and it was held by Sa'id ibn Jubayr, 'Ata', and al-Awza'i.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a detailed presentation of four scholarly positions on how to handle doubt (shakk) in the number of rak'at performed during prayer. It exemplifies how differences in the interpretation and preference of hadith lead to divergent fiqh rulings. The first position, attributed to several Companions and Tabi'un and adopted by the Zahiriyya, requires two prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdat al-sahw) when in doubt, without specifying on which number to build. Their evidence includes the hadith of Abu Hurairah and Ibn Mas'ud. The second position is that of Imam Ahmad, who distinguishes between the imam and the ma'mum, with two narrations on each: for the imam, he builds on predominant probability (ghalabat al-dhann) or on certainty, and for the individual or ma'mum, he builds on certainty or also on predominant probability. The third position is Abu Hanifah's: if the doubt occurs for the first time, the prayer is invalid because he must have certainty; if the doubt is habitual (i.e., the person frequently suffers from waswas), he builds on predominant probability through investigation (taharrī), and if no predominant probability exists, he builds on the minimum (e.g., if unsure between three and four, he assumes three). This is based on the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, which orders building on the lower number and prostrating before salam. Ibn Hazm severely criticized this view. The fourth position, held by several prominent Companions and early scholars, is to simply build on certainty (yastayqin), i.e., take the lower number. This passage shows how the same general issue yields different rulings based on which hadith is given priority and how it is interpreted—a key theme in the book's thesis that differences in sanad and matn cause juristic differences.

Key Terms
شك (shakk): Doubt in the number of rak'at performed during prayer
غالب الظن (ghalabat al-dhann): Predominant probability, what one strongly supposes to be correct after investigation
تحري (taharrī): The act of exerting one's reasoning to determine the most likely correct ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, what should a person do if he has a one-time doubt about whether he prayed three or four rak'at?
A) Build on predominant probability
B) Build on three rak'at and perform sajdat al-sahw
C) His prayer is invalid and must be repeated
D) Build on four rak'at

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between first-time and habitual doubt in prayer demonstrates how contextual interpretation of hadith leads to unique rulings within the school.
