Acceptance of Ziyadat al-Thiqa: The Case of the Hunting Dog Exception
Examination of the addition 'except for hunting dog' and its impact on the prohibition of selling dogs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for ziyadat al-thiqa to evaluate whether an addition in a hadith is accepted, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf in the ruling on selling dogs and cats.

Core Idea
Ziyadat al-thiqa is accepted unless it contradicts a stronger source—here, the addition 'except hunting dog' resolves a fiqh dispute.

This passage examines a usul dispute over whether an addition (ziyada) made by a trustworthy narrator should be accepted. The addition 'except hunting dog' appears only in Hammad ibn Salama's narration of the hadith prohibiting the sale of dogs and cats. Acceptance of this addition would exempt hunting dogs from the prohibition. This case illustrates how usul principles—specifically the rule of ziyadat al-thiqa—directly impact fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the addition and its source
Locate the addition 'except hunting dog' in the hadith narrated by Hammad ibn Salama from Abu al-Zubayr from Jabir. Note that Hammad is considered trustworthy (thiqah).
The addition is a ziyada by a reliable narrator.
2
Examine the marfu' vs. mawquf dispute
Observe that Hammad's narration is transmitted both as marfu' (raised to the Prophet) and mawquf (stopped at Jabir), with one narration expressing doubt. This dispute affects the strength of the hadith as evidence.
Multiple narrators support the marfu' version.
3
Analyze the contradictory narrations
Note that Ma'qil ibn Ubayd Allah and Ibn Lahi'a narrate the same hadith from Abu al-Zubayr without the addition. This contradiction challenges the acceptance of the addition.
Other chains also omit the addition.
4
Apply Hanafi rule on ziyadat al-thiqa
In Hanafi usul, an addition by a trustworthy narrator is generally accepted if it does not contradict a stronger source. Here, the addition is supported by multiple chains but also contradicted. Weigh the strength of narrators and consistency.
The Hanafi position traditionally accepts the addition, making hunting dogs permissible.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the addition is accepted, selling hunting dogs is permissible; if rejected, all dogs are prohibited. This directly affects fatwa.
This shows the connection between usul and fiqh.

النموذج الثاني
ما رواه حماد بن سلمة، عن أبي الزبير عن جابر: «أنه نهى عن ثمن الكلب والسنَّوْرِ، إلا كلب الصيد».
وردت هذه الزيادة إلا كلب صيد في حديث حماد بن سلمة، عن أبي الزبير عن جابر بن عبد الله، وحماد ثقة).
إلا أنه اختلف عليه في رفعه ووقفه.
فقد رواه عن حماد مرفوعاً كل من (أبي نعيم )، سويد بن عمرو)، وحجاج ابن محمد ()، والهيثم بن جميل (۲) جميعهم رووه مرفوعاً، وفيه ذكر الزيادة. (۱) ورواه عبد الواحد بن غياث (۳) ، عن حماد موقوفاً، وفيه ذكر الزيادة.
ورواه عبيد الله بن موسى (4)، بالشك عن حماد، وفيه ذكر الزيادة. 
٣٦٧
ومع اتساع الخلاف في رواية حماد فقد خولف حماد في روايته للزيادة. فقد خالفه معقل بن عبيد الله ()، وابن لهيعة (٢) كلاهما عن أبي الزبير، عن جابر بدون ذكر الزيادة.
وللحديث طرق أخرى عن جابر بدون ذكر الزيادة:
The second model: What Hammad ibn Salama narrated, from Abu al-Zubayr from Jabir: 'He forbade the price of a dog and a cat, except the hunting dog.' This addition 'except hunting dog' appears in the hadith of Hammad ibn Salama, from Abu al-Zubayr from Jabir ibn Abd Allah, and Hammad is trustworthy. Except that there is disagreement on its being marfu' and mawquf. For it was narrated from Hammad as marfu' by everyone from (Abu Nu'aym), Suwaid ibn Amr, and Hajjaj ibn Muhammad, and al-Haytham ibn Jamil — all of them narrated it as marfu', and in it is the mention of the addition. And Abd al-Wahid ibn Ghayath narrated it from Hammad as mawquf, and in it is the mention of the addition. And Ubayd Allah ibn Musa narrated it from Hammad with doubt, and in it is the mention of the addition. Despite the extensive disagreement in the narration of Hammad, Hammad was contradicted in his narration of the addition. For Ma'qil ibn Ubayd Allah and Ibn Lahi'a both narrated from Abu al-Zubayr from Jabir without mentioning the addition. And there are other chains from Jabir without mentioning the addition.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage details a usul dispute concerning the acceptance of an addition (ziyada) by a trustworthy narrator. The addition 'except hunting dog' appears only in Hammad's narration. However, Hammad's narration is disputed as to whether it is marfu' (raised to the Prophet) or mawquf (stopped at Jabir). Furthermore, other narrators contradict Hammad by omitting the addition. This affects the fiqh ruling on selling dogs and cats: if the addition is accepted, the sale of hunting dogs is exempted from the general prohibition. The Hanafi position traditionally is that the hadith is authentic and the exception is accepted, making the sale of hunting dogs permissible, while the sale of cats and other dogs remains prohibited. The analysis here shows the usul principle that ziyadat al-thiqa is accepted if it does not contradict a stronger narrator, but the presence of contradictory reports weakens the addition.

Key Terms
زيادة الثقة (ziyadat al-thiqa): An addition to the text of a hadith made by a trustworthy narrator that is not present in other narrations.
رفع ووقف (raf' wa waqf): The distinction between a hadith being attributed to the Prophet (marfu') or to a Companion (mawquf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the source text, which of the following best describes the status of Hammad ibn Salama's narration that includes the exception for hunting dogs?
A) It is unanimously accepted as marfu'
B) It is disputed between marfu' and mawquf
C) It is rejected because of doubt in the chain
D) It is only narrated by weak narrators

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of ziyadat al-thiqa in Hanafi usul can resolve fiqh disputes, but contradictions in chains require careful evaluation.

The Ziyada Dispute: Does a Trustworthy Narrator's Addition Change the Ruling on Selling Dogs?
Analyzing a disputed hadith addition and its effect on fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the effect of a disputed hadith addition (ziyada) on fiqh rulings and apply the Hanafi usul principle for accepting or rejecting such additions.

Core Idea
A narrator's addition to a hadith is not automatically accepted; it must be weighed against the reliability of the majority.

In this lesson, we examine how a single addition (istithna) reported by Hammad ibn Zayd in the hadith prohibiting the price of a dog led to divergent rulings among the schools. We will analyze the textual criticism of the addition and see how Imam Abu Hanifa relied on istihsan rather than the narrow exception, while other scholars accepted the addition and limited the prohibition accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed addition
Locate the exact wording of Hammad's addition in the hadith of Jabir. What does it add to the original prohibition?
The addition is: 'except for the price of a trained dog' (الاستثناء في ثمن الكلب المعلم).
2
Assess the strength of the addition
Note which scholars accepted the addition and which rejected it, and their reasons. Ibn al-Turkumani accepted it as a trustworthy addition; Ibn Hibban and al-Bayhaqi rejected it because more reliable narrators omitted it.
The text provides three narrators (Abu Sufyan, 'Ata', Shurahbil) who all narrated from Jabir without the addition.
3
Trace the effect on fiqh rulings
Identify which jurists changed their positions based on the addition. Some early scholars (Jabir, Abu Hurairah, etc.) permitted sale only of hunting dogs; Imam Abu Hanifa did not accept the addition and permitted all dog sales via istihsan.
The passage shows the practical thamarah al-khilaf of the hadith dispute.
4
Compare the Hanafi position
Why did Abu Hanifa not accept the addition? His reasoning is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because dogs have beneficial uses. Abu Yusuf restricted the permission to exclude ferocious dogs.
Note that Abu Hanifa's original ruling (riwayat al-asl) permits sale of any dog, trained or not.
5
Derive the usul principle
What does this case teach about the Hanafi rule for ziyadat al-thiqah? The addition is not accepted when it contradicts more reliable narrators, and even if accepted, it may be overridden by istihsan.
This illustrates the interplay between hadith criticism and substantive legal reasoning.

الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن

فقد رواه أبو سفيان(۱) ، وعطاء (۲)، وشرحبيل (۳) ثلاثتهم عن جابر دون ذكر الزيادة، مما يدل على خطأ حماد في ذكرها إذ ليس من المعقول أن يغفل عنها الرواة في جميع الطبقات ويحفظها حماد.

إلا أن بعض العلماء يعد هذه الزيادة زيادة ثقة يتعين قبولها، فقد قال ابن التركماني: «هذا إسناد جيد، فظهر أن الحديث صحيح، والاستثناء زيادة على أحاديث النهي عن ثمن الكلب فوجب قبولها».

وقد ضعف ابن حبان هذه الزيادة فقال: «هذا الخبر بهذا اللفظ لا أصل له، ولا يجوز ثمن الكلب المعلم ولا غيره). وكذلك البيهقي فقال: «الأحاديث الصحاح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في النهي عن ثمن الكلب خالية من هذا الاستثناء، وإنما الاستثناء في الأحاديث الصحاح في النهي عن الاقتناء ولعله شبه على من ذكر في حديث النهي عن ثمنه من هؤلاء الرواة الذين هم دون الصحابة والتابعين والله أعلم).

أثر هذه الزيادة في اختلاف الفقهاء : حكم بيع الكلب المعلم

اختلف الفقهاء في حكم بيع الكلب فقد ذهبت جماعة من أهل العلم إلى جواز بيع كلب الصيد دون غيره، روي هذا عن جابر بن عبد الله، وأبي هريرة، وعطاء، وزيد بن علي، والنخعي). والحجة لهم زيادة حماد السابقة.

أما الإمام أبو حنيفة فيجوز عنده بيع الكلب معلماً كان أو غير معلم في رواية الأصل). وعن أبي يوسف) لا يجوز بيع الكلب العقور؛ واستدلوا بأن الكلب منفعة يجوز بيعه.

أما الإمام مالك فقد قال: «أكره ثمن الكلب الضاري وغير الضاري لنهي رسول الله عن ثمن الكلب).

وقد وضح ابن عبد البر ذلك فقال: «وقد اختلف أصحاب مالك واختلفت الرواية عنده في ثمن الكلب الذي أبيح اتخاذه، فأجاز مرة ثمن الكلب الضاري، ومنع منه أخرى، ووجه إجازة بيع ما أبيح اتخاذه من الكلاب؛ لأن الحديث الذي ورد بالنهي عن ثمن الكلب، فمن نذر معه حلوان الكاهن ومهر البغي وهذا لا يباح شيء منه على أنه الكلب الذي لا يجوز اتخاذه والله أعلم؛ لأن من الكلاب ما أبيح اتخاذه، والانتفاع به، فذلك جائز بيعه».

وعند الإمام مالك أن من قتل كلب صيد أو ماشية أو زرع فعليه قيمته). وذهب أكثر أهل العلم إلى أنه لا يجوز بيع الكلب سواء كان معلماً أو غير معلم، ولا ضمان على متلفه.
Chapter Three: Disagreement in Chain and Text. Abu Sufyan, Ata, and Shurahbil all narrated from Jabir without mentioning the addition, which indicates that Hammad's mention of it is mistaken, since it is inconceivable that the narrators in all levels would omit it while Hammad preserved it. However, some scholars consider this addition to be a trustworthy addition that must be accepted. Ibn al-Turkumani said: 'This chain is good, so the hadith is authentic, and the exception is an addition to the hadiths prohibiting the price of a dog, so it must be accepted.' Ibn Hibban weakened this addition, saying: 'This report in this wording has no basis, and the price of a trained dog or any dog is not permissible.' Likewise, al-Bayhaqi said: 'The authentic hadiths from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the prohibition of the price of a dog are free of this exception. Rather, the exception occurs in the authentic hadiths regarding the prohibition of keeping a dog, and it may have been confused for those who mentioned it in the hadith prohibiting its price from among those narrators below the Companions and Followers. And Allah knows best.' Effect of this addition on the disagreement of jurists: The ruling on selling a trained dog. The jurists differed regarding the ruling on selling a dog. A group of scholars held the permissibility of selling a hunting dog only. This is reported from Jabir ibn Abdullah, Abu Hurairah, Ata, Zayd ibn Ali, and al-Nakha'i. Their evidence is Hammad's addition mentioned earlier. As for Imam Abu Hanifah, he permits the sale of a dog, whether trained or untrained, in the original narration. From Abu Yusuf, it is not permissible to sell a ferocious dog; and they argued that the dog has a benefit that makes its sale permissible. As for Imam Malik, he said: 'I dislike the price of a predatory dog and a non-predatory dog due to the Prophet's prohibition of the price of a dog.' Ibn Abd al-Barr clarified this, saying: 'Malik's companions differed and the narration from him differed concerning the price of a dog that it is permissible to keep. He once permitted the price of a predatory dog, and another time forbade it. The basis for permitting the sale of what it is permissible to keep from dogs is that the hadith that came prohibiting the price of a dog—together with the soothsayer's fee and the prostitute's wage—none of which are permissible—indicates that it refers to the dog that is not permissible to keep. And Allah knows best, because among dogs are those that it is permissible to keep and benefit from, so their sale is permissible.' According to Imam Malik, whoever kills a hunting dog, a herding dog, or a farming dog must pay its value. And most scholars hold that selling a dog is not permissible whether trained or untrained, and there is no liability on the one who destroys it.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a case study in usul al-fiqh showing how a disputed addition (ziyada) in a hadith leads to differing fiqh rulings. It opens with the textual criticism: Hammad's addition of an exception (istithna) for trained dogs is rejected by some scholars because more reliable narrators omitted it, while others accept it as a trustworthy addition. The author then traces the effect: some early jurists (including Companions) permitted sale only of hunting dogs based on this addition. Abu Hanifa, however, in the original narration (riwayat al-asl), allows sale of any dog, whether trained or not, based on istihsan (juristic preference) because dogs have beneficial use. Abu Yusuf restricts this by excluding ferocious dogs. Malik expresses dislike but his followers differ, with some permitting sale of dogs that may be kept (e.g., hunting dogs). The majority opinion forbids all dog sales. The passage thus illustrates how authenticity assessment of a hadith text directly shapes the resulting fiqh positions.

Key Terms
زيادة الثقة (ziyadat al-thiqah): An addition to a hadith made by a trustworthy narrator, which some scholars accept as authentic.
كلب معلم (kalb muallim): A dog trained for hunting, guarding livestock, or farming.
استثناء (istithna): An exception made in a general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He accepts Hammad's addition as authentic.
B) He relies on istihsan (juristic preference) based on the dog's benefit.
C) He follows a different hadith that explicitly allows all dog sales.
D) He considers the prohibition to be abrogated.

Key Takeaway
A narrator's addition to a hadith is not automatically accepted; it must be weighed against the reliability of the majority, and even when textual evidence is weak, istihsan may produce a different ruling.

Dispute over Iqama Wording: Three Opinions and the Abrogation Claim
Three opinions on iqama wording and abrogation debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the iqama wording, state the Hanafi position, and evaluate the abrogation claim using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
The iqama wording dispute shows how different hadith chains lead to valid methodological ikhtilaf in fiqh rulings.

The iqama, the second call to prayer, has varying wordings reported from the Companions. This passage presents three schools on how many times each phrase is repeated. At the heart of the disagreement is whether Abu Mahdhura's hadith abrogates the earlier practice of Bilal. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi usul handles conflicts between ahad hadith and how the school establishes its preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
List each opinion on the iqama wording, noting which phrases are said once or twice. The first opinion claims all iqama phrases are singular except 'qad qamat al-salah' twice? Wait, check the text: first opinion: Abu Mahdhura's hadith gives 17 words, implying a specific structure; they claim it abrogates Bilal's practice. Second opinion: all phrases singular including 'qad qamat al-salah' once. Third opinion: all phrases singular except 'qad qamat al-salah' twice.
The first opinion is not explicitly described in the snippet but is inferred from the abrogation claim.
2
Examine the dalil for each opinion
For each opinion, identify the key hadith(s) cited. First: Abu Mahdhura's hadith (word count). Second: Anas's hadith 'make adhan even and iqama odd'. Third: actions of companions like Umar, Ibn Umar, Anas, and narrations from Bilal and Abu Mahdhura.
3
Analyze the abrogation claim
The first opinion claims naskh by Abu Mahdhura's hadith. Evaluate the strength of this claim using Hanafi usul principles: conditions for naskh (e.g., later timing, strength of evidence). Note that Ahmad ibn Hanbal rejected the claim citing the Prophet's approval of Bilal's practice after returning to Medina.
In Hanafi usul, naskh of a known practice by a ahad hadith is generally weak.
4
Determine the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school follows the third opinion. Understand why based on companion practice and the flexibility in the transmitted texts. The passage indicates many scholars preferred this view.
The third opinion allows both double and single wording except 'qad qamat al-salah' which is twice in any case.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is how many times 'qad qamat al-salah' is said in the iqama: once (second opinion) or twice (third opinion, including Hanafi).

Chapter Three: Disagreement in Sanad and Matn. The first opinion: The Prophet taught him the adhan with nineteen words and the iqama with seventeen words, and they held that the hadith of Abu Mahdhura abrogates the hadith of Bilal. The second opinion: It holds that the words of the iqama are singular, and his saying 'qad qamat al-salah' is once also. This is the opinion of al-Layth ibn Sa'd and Malik, and al-Mawardi said: al-Shafi'i said it in his old opinion. And they argued with the hadith of Anas: 'He ordered Bilal to make the adhan even and the iqama odd.' The third opinion: They said: The wording of the iqama is singular except for his saying 'qad qamat al-salah' for he says it twice, and it is narrated from the action of some companions including 'Umar, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, Anas, and it is a narration from Bilal and Abu Mahdhura, may Allah be pleased with them, and this is what 'Urwa ibn al-Zubayr, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, al-Hasan, Muhammad ibn Sirin, Makhul, al-Zuhri, al-Awza'i, al-Shafi'i, Ibn Hazm al-Zahiri favored this opinion, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Dawud ibn 'Ali al-Zahiri, and Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari preferred this, except they allowed the iqama to be double or single except 'qad qamat al-salah' is twice in any case, and this is what Ibn 'Abd al-Barr indicated. Ibn Hajar said: Ahmad disapproved of those who claimed abrogation by the hadith of Abu Mahdhura, and argued that the Prophet returned to Medina and approved Bilal's individual iqama and taught Sa'd al-Qaraz to give it after him as narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Hakim.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the legal status of the iqama wording based on differences in hadith texts and chains. It presents three main positions. The first opinion holds that Abu Mahdhura's hadith, which gives specific numbers of words, abrogates the earlier practice of Bilal. The second opinion maintains the iqama phrases are all singular, including 'qad qamat al-salah' once, based on Anas's hadith. The third opinion, attributed to many major scholars, holds that all iqama phrases are singular except 'qad qamat al-salah' which is repeated twice, and that this is flexible. Ibn Hajar then reports Ahmad ibn Hanbal's rejection of the abrogation claim, citing that the Prophet approved Bilal's practice after returning to Medina. The dispute hinges on whether Abu Mahdhura's hadith is abrogating (nasikh) or not. Hanafi scholars generally follow the view that iqama is individual except 'qad qamat al-salah' twice, as is the established practice.

Key Terms
إقامة (iqama): The second call to prayer, said immediately before the obligatory prayer begins.
ناسخ (nasikh): A later revelation or ruling that abrogates an earlier one.
نَسْخ (naskh): The legal principle of abrogation, where a later divine ruling supersedes an earlier one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three times
D) Not said at all

Key Takeaway
Abrogation claims between ahad hadiths require strong evidence of chronological order and are often contested; companion practice can serve as a stronger basis for the ruling.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Iqama Wording Dispute and Rejection of Abrogation
Summary of scholarly positions and rejection of abrogation claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ikhtilaf over iqama wording and evaluate the Hanafi usul response to the naskh claim.

Core Idea
The Prophet's continuing practice of Bilal's iqama refutes the abrogation claim.

The wording of the iqama is a point of difference among early scholars. Some allowed both paired and single forms, except for 'qad qamat al-salah' which must be repeated. Others argued that the hadith of Abu Mahdhura abrogated the earlier practice. This lesson examines the dispute and the Hanafi response, which appeals to continuity of practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf
List the two main positions on iqama wording: paired vs. flexible (except one phrase).
2
Locate the naskh claim
Understand the claim that the hadith of Abu Mahdhura abrogates the earlier iqama form.
Abrogation requires clear timeline evidence.
3
Analyze Ahmad's argument
Examine the argument: Prophet returned to Medina and kept Bilal's iqama, teaching it to Sa'd al-Qarad.
This is a dalil naqli against naskh.
4
Apply to Hanafi position
Connect Ahmad's reasoning to the Hanafi school's preference for fixed paired iqama.
Hanafi usul: amal (practice) can outweigh a solitary hadith.
5
Determine thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical impact: Hanafi doubles all phrases; others permit both forms.

الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن
والحسن (۱)، ومحمد بن سيرين (۳)، ومكحول (۳) ، والزهري)، والأوزاعي)، (V) والشافعي (٦) ، وابن حزم الظاهري ) وفضل هذا المذهب إسحاق بن راهويه )، وأحمد بن حنبل)، وداود بن علي (۱۱) الظاهري (١٠) ، ومحمد بن جرير الطبري (١) ، إلا أنهم أجازوا أن تكون الإقامة مثنى مثنى أو إفرادها إلا قد قامت الصلاة فإنها مرتان على كل حال وهذا ما أشار إليه ابن عبد البر (۱۲). قال ابن حجر: «وقد أنكر أحمد على من ادعى النسخ بحديث أبي محذورة، واحتج بأن النبي الله رجع إلى المدينة وأقرّ بلالاً على إفراده الإقامة وعلمه سعد القرظ فأذن به بعده كما رواه الدارقطني والحاكم (١٣).
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. Al-Hasan (1), Muhammad ibn Sirin (2), Mak-hul (3), al-Zuhri (4), al-Awza'i (5), al-Shafi'i (6), Ibn Hazm al-Zahiri (7), and this school was favored by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh (8), Ahmad ibn Hanbal (9), Dawud ibn Ali al-Zahiri (10), and Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari (11), except that they permitted the iqama to be repeated twice or single, except for 'qad qamat al-salah' which is twice in any case, and this is what Ibn Abd al-Barr indicated (12). Ibn Hajar said: 'Ahmad rejected those who claimed abrogation by the hadith of Abu Mahdhura, and argued that the Prophet (peace be upon him) returned to Medina and kept Bilal on his single iqama, and taught Sa'd al-Qarad to give the adhan after him as narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Hakim (13).'
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a survey of scholarly positions on the wording of the iqama (the second call to prayer). The author lists a number of early scholars including al-Hasan al-Basri, Ibn Sirin, etc. and later jurists like al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and Dawud al-Zahiri, all of whom allowed both saying the iqama in pairs (muthanna) or singly (ifrad), except for the phrase 'qad qamat al-salah' which must be recited twice in all cases. This is the view also reported by Ibn Abd al-Barr. Then the author quotes Ibn Hajar's statement that Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal rejected the claim of abrogation (naskh) regarding the hadith of Abu Mahdhura (which indicates a different iqama), and Ahmad used the argument that the Prophet (peace be upon him) returned to Medina and maintained Bilal's iqama as it was (single), and taught Sa'd al-Qarad to use that form. This is a clear example of a substantive disagreement (ikhtilaf) among scholars that stems from differences in how they weigh the evidence from the hadith chain and text. The Hanafi school, for its part, holds that the iqama is to be recited in pairs based on the practice of Bilal as reported in authentic hadith, and does not accept the claim of abrogation by the hadith of Abu Mahdhura because the practice continued after the Prophet's return. This passage shows that many scholars opposed the view that only one form is valid, and they allowed flexibility.

Key Terms
الإقامة (al-iqama): The second call to commence the congregational prayer
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a previous ruling by later revelation or sunna

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the phrase 'qad qamat al-salah' in the iqama?
A) It must be said twice in all cases.
B) It can be said once or twice.
C) It must be said only once.
D) It is not part of the iqama.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on iqama is based on the established practice of Bilal and rejects the naskh claim due to evidence of continuity after the Prophet's return.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Hand Placement in Prayer: A Case Study of Ikhtilaf
Presents two madhhab positions on hand placement in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two major positions on hand placement in prayer, state the scholars associated with each, and explain why this ikhtilaf is a legitimate expression of diversity in fiqh.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf in hand placement reflects different transmissions, not lack of evidence.

This passage presents a classic case of ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) in fiqh: where should a person place their hands during the standing posture in prayer? The Hanafi school places them under the navel, while the majority (Jumhur) places them above the navel and below the chest. This difference is not arbitrary; it stems from different hadith narrations and the weight given to companion practice. By studying this case, you will understand how usul al-fiqh principles, especially the evaluation of hadith chains and texts, directly influence practical rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Read the passage and note the two positions: under navel (Hanafi) and above navel below chest (Jumhur).
These are the two main views.
2
List the supporters
For each position, list the scholars and companions mentioned. For the first: Abu Huraira, Anas, Ali (in one narration), Abu Hanifa, Ahmad (in one narration), Sufyan al-Thawri, etc. For the second: Jumhur, al-Nawawi, Sa'id ibn Jubayr.
Note that the first position includes early Kufan scholars, the second is more widespread.
3
Analyze the authority
Consider that both positions cite authoritative figures. The Hanafi position relies on companions and early imams; the Jumhur position relies on the consensus of later scholars.
This shows ikhtilaf is not from ignorance but from different evaluations of evidence.
4
Connect to usul
Understand that this ikhtilaf is rooted in differences in hadith criticism (rijal) and the prioritization of companion practice (qawl al-sahabi) versus general hadith texts.
This is a classic example of how asanid and mutun affect fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The effect of the hadith on the difference among jurists regarding the placement of the hands when standing in prayer. The jurists differed in that into two schools: The first school: the hands are placed under the navel. Commentary
This passage is a case study showing how differences in hadith chains and texts lead to differences in fiqh rulings. The specific issue is the placement of the hands (sunnah position) during standing in prayer. The author presents the two main positions: First, the hands are placed under the navel, which is the Hanafi position supported by several companions and early scholars. Second, the hands are placed above the navel and below the chest, which is the position of the majority of scholars (Jumhur) including the Shafi'i school. The passage lists notable names for each position to illustrate the weight of the ikhtilaf. Note that the Hanafi position is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifah and other early Kufan scholars, while the majority position is attributed to the Jumhur and later scholars like al-Nawawi.

Key Terms
موضع اليدين (mawdi' al-yadayn): The ruling on where to place the hands during the standing posture in prayer.
تحت السرة (tahta al-surra): Below the navel; the Hanafi position for hand placement.
فوق السرة وتحت الصدر (fawqa al-surra wa tahta al-sadr): Above the navel and below the chest; the position of the majority (Jumhur).

Key Takeaway
The placement of hands in prayer is a classic example of how legitimate ikhtilaf arises from different hadith transmissions and scholarly evaluations, not from lack of evidence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Effect of Sanad and Matn Differences on Fiqh: Hand Placement in Prayer
Lists four madhabs on hand placement and critiques a narration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how differences in hadith transmission (sanad and matn) lead to different fiqh rulings among schools, using the case of hand placement in prayer.

Core Idea
Hadith transmission variance, not just text, shapes madhhab rulings — as seen in the four opinions on hand placement.

This passage demonstrates a fundamental usul al-fiqh principle: ikhtilaf in fiqh often originates from ikhtilaf in hadith transmission (sanad and matn). The author critiques a specific narration that contradicts the known position of its narrator, Sufyan al-Thawri. He then enumerates the four distinct positions on where to place the hands during prayer. By examining these schools, we see how variations in the strength and interpretation of hadiths lead to multiple valid rulings within the Ummah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul issue
The passage highlights how a difference in the sanad (a narrator's addition) is rejected because it contradicts the known fiqh position of the narrator himself. This illustrates a usul principle: when a narrator's riwayah conflicts with his own known ra'y, the riwayah is deemed weak (or the addition is unacceptable).
2
Identify the four madhhab positions
List each school's ruling on hand placement and which scholars uphold it: (1) Under navel (Hanafi, etc.), (2) Above navel below chest (Shafi'i, etc.), (3) Option between both (Ahmad third view), (4) Let hands hang (some early scholars).
3
Trace the textual basis
For each position, infer which hadith or principle it is based on. The Hanafi position relies on reports from Companions and the practice of the Salaf; the Shafi'i position relies on a narration of Wail ibn Hujr, etc.
The passage does not cite specific hadiths, but we can infer from context and footnotes.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author argues that an addition (placing hands on chest) is invalid because it lacks support from reliable narrations and from the practice of the Salaf. He specifically points out that the addition contradicts Sufyan al-Thawri's own position.
5
Derive the usul principle
The lesson's underlying usul rule: 'A hadith that contradicts the established practice (amal) of the Salaf or the known position of a reliable narrator may be considered shadh (irregular) and therefore not acted upon.' This principle is applied by Hanafis in evaluating riwayah.

Chapter Three: Disagreement in Sanad and Matn without mentioning the addition. 381 So this addition at this extreme of opposition cannot be accepted, especially since the basis of Mu'ammal's addition is Sufyan al-Thawri, and the school of Sufyan on this matter is placing the hands under the navel. If this addition were established from his path, he would not have opposed it. Added to this is that I have not found a strong transmission from any of the Salaf saying to place the right hand over the left on the chest; so it is also an addition contradicted by the lack of practice of the people of knowledge. And Allah knows best. The effect of hadith in the disagreement of jurists: the position of the hands during standing in prayer. The jurists differed on that into schools: The first school: the two hands are placed under the navel. This was held by Abu Hurairah, Anas ibn Malik, and Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib in one narration from him – may Allah be pleased with them all. And it is the school of Imam Abu Hanifah, and Ahmad in one narration from him, and Sufyan al-Thawri, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, and Abu Ishaq among the followers of al-Shafi'i, and Abu Mijlaz, and al-Nakha'i. The second school: they are placed above the navel and below the chest. This is the school of the majority, as stated by al-Nawawi - may Allah have mercy on him - and it was held by Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and al-Shafi'i, and it is a narration from Malik, and a narration from Ahmad. Rather it is another narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib, as al-Nawawi said. The third school: option: under the navel or above it, and it is a third statement of Imam Ahmad, and it is the school of al-Awza'i, and 'Ata', and Ibn al-Mundhir. And Ibn Habib said: There is no known specific position for that. The fourth school: letting them hang. This is the school of Ibn al-Zubayr, and al-Hasan al-Basri, and al-Nakha'i, according to what Ibn al-Mundhir reported from them, and it is also reported from Ibn Sirin. (Footnotes omitted for brevity in translation)
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents a classic example of how differences in hadith transmission (sanad and matn) lead to differences in fiqh rulings among schools. The author first critiques a particular narration (that of Mu'ammal from Sufyan al-Thawri) because it contradicts the known position of Sufyan himself, thus arguing that the addition is unreliable. He then lists the four distinct opinions on the placement of the hands during prayer (qiyam). The first opinion, placing the hands under the navel, is attributed to several Companions and is the Hanafi school's position. The second opinion, above the navel and below the chest, is attributed to the majority and to al-Shafi'i. The third opinion gives latitude for either position. The fourth opinion is to let the hands hang freely. This enumeration shows that the ikhtilaf is well-established and stems from different narrations and interpretations.

Key Terms
السرة (al-surra): The navel; used as a reference point for hand placement in prayer.
الإرسال (al-irsal): Letting the hands hang freely at the sides during prayer, without folding them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which hand placement is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifah?
A) Above the navel and below the chest
B) Under the navel
C) Option between under and above
D) Letting the hands hang

Key Takeaway
Differences in hadith transmission (sanad and matn) are a primary cause of ikhtilaf among jurists; the Hanafi school employs the principle of 'amal al-rawi to evaluate narrations.

The Third, Fourth, and Fifth Schools on Hand Placement: A Case Study in Ikhtilaf
Enumerating three schools on hand placement with proponents and rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third, fourth, and fifth schools of hand placement in prayer, state their rulings and proponents, and explain how differences in hadith chains and texts lead to this ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Different hadith chains and texts produce different fiqh rulings for the same act of prayer.

In previous lessons, we encountered the first two schools on hand placement (below navel and above navel). The present passage continues the survey by introducing three more positions: the option (takhyir), letting the hands hang (irsal), and placing on the chest. Each of these is attributed to various companions, successors, and imams, and the author notes that some attributions are not authentic. This passage is a classic example of how ikhtilaf in fiqh stems from differences in the chains (asanid) and texts (mutun) of hadith.

Guided Path
1
List the three schools
Read the passage and identify the third, fourth, and fifth schools mentioned. Write down their key rulings in one sentence each.
The third school is takhyir (choice between below or above navel), the fourth is irsal (letting hang), the fifth is placing on the chest.
2
Identify the proponents of each school
For each school, note the names of the scholars or groups attributed to it. Pay attention to the author's comments on the authenticity of these attributions.
For example, the chest placement is attributed to Ali, al-Shafi'i, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, but the author says these attributions are not authentic.
3
Analyze internal subdivisions within a school
Within the fourth school (irsal), there are further differences. Identify the views of al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya versus the Hadiyya regarding the effect of placing the right hand on the left.
Al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya say it is makruh but prayer remains valid; the Hadiyya say it invalidates the prayer.
4
Observe the author's authentication remarks
Notice where the passage casts doubt on attributions. What does the author say about the chest placement school? What does Ibn Habib say about takhyir?
Ibn Habib said there is no known place for takhyir. The author states that attributing chest placement to Ali, al-Shafi'i, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh is not authentic.
5
Summarize the lesson on ikhtilaf
In one or two sentences, explain how this passage demonstrates that differences in hadith chains and texts lead to differing fiqh rulings.
The multiplicity of opinions on hand placement arises because each school relies on different hadith evidence or interprets the same hadith differently.

المذهب الثالث: التخيير: تحت السرة أو فوقها وهو قول ثالث للإمام أحمد، وهو مذهب الأوزاعي وعطاء وابن المنذر (۸).
وقال ابن حبيب: ليس لذلك موضع معروف.

المذهب الخامس: توضعان على الصدر.
نسبه القرطبي للإمام علي (١٥) ولا يصح عنه، ونسبه المرغيناني للشافعي (١٤) ولا يصح عنه، ونسبه الألباني لإسحاق بن راهويه (١٦) ولا يصح عنه، وهذا المذهب اختاره الصنعاني والمباركفوري وصاحب "عون (۱۸).
Chapter Three / Disagreement in Chain and Text. And al-Shafi'i (1), and it is a narration from Malik (2), and a narration from Ahmad (3). Rather, it is another narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib, as al-Nawawi said. The third school: choice: below the navel or above it, and it is a third saying of Imam Ahmad, and it is the school of al-Awza'i, Ata, and Ibn al-Mundhir (8). And Ibn Habib said: There is no known place for that. The fourth school: letting hang. It is the school of Ibn al-Zubayr, al-Hasan al-Basri (11), and al-Nakha'i (12), according to what Ibn al-Mundhir narrated from them (13), and it is also narrated from Ibn Sirin (18). And it is the school of Malik (1) in one narration from him, in the famous of his school, otherwise the transmission from him concerning this has become confused. And it is the school of al-Layth ibn Sa'd (3), and Ibn J (3), and Ibn Jurayj (4), and Ata, and al-Qasimiyya, and al-Nasiriyya, and al-Baqir (10). We still have to say that al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya (10) adopted the saying of letting hang, with their statement that it is disliked to place the right hand on the left but the prayer is not invalidated if one does so. As for the Hadiyya (11), they adopted the saying of letting hang, and that the prayer is invalidated if one places his right hand on his left in prayer. Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a listing of three different schools of thought (madhahib) on the placement of hands during prayer, illustrating how differences in hadith chains and texts lead to divergent rulings. The author continues from earlier pages where the first two schools (likely below navel and above navel) were discussed. The third school (al-madhhab al-thalith) is the option of choosing between placing hands below or above the navel (takhyir), attributed to Imam Ahmad in one of his three opinions, and also to al-Awza'i, 'Ata, and Ibn al-Mundhir. Ibn Habib, however, stated that there is no fixed position for this. The fourth school (al-madhhab al-rabi') is that of letting the hands hang freely (irsal), which is reported from several companions, successors, and imams, including Ibn al-Zubayr, al-Hasan al-Basri, al-Nakha'i, Ibn Sirin, and others. The author notes that Malik also held this view in one narration, but there is confusion in the transmissions from him. Within this school, further subdivisions are mentioned: al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya (Zaydi imams) held it is makruh (disliked) to place the right on the left, but the prayer remains valid, while the Hadiyya school (another Zaydi group) held that it invalidates the prayer. The fifth school (al-madhhab al-khamis) is placing both hands on the chest, which the author notes is attributed to Imam 'Ali, al-Shafi'i, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, but states that none of these attributions are authentic. This opinion was later chosen by scholars like al-San'ani and al-Mubarakfuri. The passage demonstrates that the same issue (wad' al-yad) has multiple rulings due to differences in the strength and interpretation of the evidence; this is a classic example of ikhtilaf resulting from variations in hadith chains (asanid) and texts (mutun).

Key Terms
الإرسال (al-irsāl): To let the hands hang freely at one's sides during prayer without clasping or placing one over the other.
التخيير (al-takhyīr): The opinion that one may choose to place the hands either below the navel or above it, both being permissible.
وضع اليدين على الصدر (waḍʿ al-yadayn ʿalā al-ṣadr): Placing the right hand over the left on the chest during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya on one hand, and the Hadiyya on the other, regarding the ruling on placing the right hand on the left in prayer?
A) Al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya say it is permissible without dislike, while the Hadiyya say it is forbidden.
B) Al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya say it is disliked but the prayer is still valid, while the Hadiyya say it invalidates the prayer.
C) Al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya say it is recommended, while the Hadiyya say it is obligatory.
D) Al-Mu'ayyad billah and Imam Yahya say it is invalid, while the Hadiyya say it is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of hand placement rulings shows that ikhtilaf arises from differences in hadith transmission and interpretation, not from arbitrary disagreement.

Hand Placement and the Usul of Attribution: A Study in Ikhtilaf
Compilation of opinions with attribution critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how differences in chains and texts generate ikhtilaf among jurists, and evaluate the reliability of attributions using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
A ruling's authority depends on its chain; weak attributions produce disputed positions.

This passage presents a survey of hand placement rulings, but its real lesson is in usul: how do we know which position is reliably attributed to which scholar? The author critiques several attributions as unauthentic, showing that ikhtilaf often originates from textual and transmission weaknesses. As a Hanafi usuli, you must distinguish between well-attested narrations and weak ones to determine the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of positions
Read the passage and list every group or individual mentioned along with their ruling on hand placement (sadl, qabd, invalidation, dislike, etc.).
Focus on both the ruling and the source attribution.
2
Distinguish substantiated from weak attributions
The author explicitly marks some attributions as unauthentic (e.g., 'لا يصح عنه'). Identify which attributions are criticized and note the reason given (only that it is not authentic).
The critique is based on chain weakness, not necessarily the ruling itself.
3
Apply the usul principle of thubut
In Hanafi usul, a ruling's authority requires a sound chain (thubut). Here, attributions to al-Shafi'i, Imam Ali, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh are rejected due to weak chains. This illustrates how ikhtilaf arises from differing assessments of riwayaat.
The Hanafi school relies on its own well-attested narrations for the practice of qabd.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Despite the lists, the practical consequence (thamarah) is that the Hanafi school follows qabd based on strong hadith. The disputed attributions do not affect the mufta bihi, but they show the diversity of opinion.
This demonstrates that not all ikhtilaf affects practice; some remains at the theoretical level.

Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents a survey of different juristic positions on the placement of hands during prayer, highlighting how differences in chains and texts (as is the book's theme) lead to varied rulings. The passage first notes Imam Malik's position (letting the hands hang) but acknowledges conflicting narrations from him. It then lists supporters of the sadl (hanging) position: al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Ibn Jurayj, 'Ata', and among the Zaydi Imams, al-Mu'ayyad Billah and Imam Yahya (who dislike the qabd but do not invalidate the prayer), while the Hadawiyya (a Zaydi sect) consider qabd as invalidating prayer. The 'fifth school' (after the four Sunni schools?) places the hands on the chest. The passage then critiques several attributions: al-Marghinani's ascription to al-Shafi'i, al-Qurtubi's to 'Ali, and al-Albani's to Ishaq ibn Rahawayh are all deemed unauthentic. Finally, it notes that al-San'ani, al-Mubarakfuri, and al-Shawkani adopted this chest-placing position.

Key Terms
الإرسال (al-irsal): The practice of letting the hands hang freely at the sides during prayer, not folding them.
الهادوية (al-Hadawiyya): A Zaydi sect named after Imam Hadi, who consider placing right hand on left during prayer as invalidating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Effect of Hadith Discrepancy on Ruling of Sujūd al-Sahw
Demonstrates how a disputed hadith addition affects fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disputed ziyāda in a hadith chain, explain its effect on the ruling of sujūd al-sahw, and evaluate the Hanafi position in light of textual criticism.

Core Idea
A disputed addition in a hadith chain directly determines whether tashahhud is required in sujūd al-sahw.

This passage presents a case study in which a single addition (ziyāda) transmitted by one narrator, Ash'ath al-Hamrani, creates a fundamental disagreement among jurists about the ruling of sujūd al-sahw. You will see how the Hanafi school, relying on the practice of companions like Ibn Mas'ud, accepts the tashahhud, while others reject it due to the weakness of the narration. By understanding this example, you will learn how usul al-hadith directly impacts fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key textual discrepancy
In the hadith about sujūd al-sahw, one narrator (Ash'ath) adds the phrase 'ذكر التشهد' (mention of tashahhud) while many reliable narrators omit it. This is the disputed ziyāda.
2
Analyze the hadith criticism
Examine the statements of al-Bayhaqi, al-'Ala'i, and Ibn 'Abd al-Barr: they argue the addition is weak because Ash'ath is less precise and contradicted by stronger narrators. Understand the principles of accepting/rejecting ziyāda.
The Hanafi usul principle: a ziyāda from a trustworthy narrator is accepted unless it contradicts a stronger narration.
3
Map the fiqh disagreement
Notice three groups: (1) no tashahhud and no salām, (2) only salām, (3) both tashahhud and salām. The Hanafi school adopts the third position.
The Hanafi position is attributed to Abu Hanifa explicitly in the text.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa bases his ruling on the practice of 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and others, not solely on the weak hadith. The school regards the practice as sufficient evidence.
This illustrates how Hanafi usul uses qawl al-sahabi (companion statement) when hadith is weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن عن وهشيم بن بشير جميعهم عن خالد الحذاء، به دون ذكر الزيادة. قال البيهقي: «تفرد به أشعث الحمراني وقد رواه شعبة ووهيب وابن علية والثقفي، وهشيم، وحماد بن زيد، ويزيد بن زريع وغيرهم عن خالد الحذاء لم يذكر أحد منهم ما ذكر أشعث عنه، ورواه أيوب، عن محمد قال: أخبرت عن عمران فذكر السلام دون التشهد، وفي رواية هشيم ذكر التشهد قبل سجدتين وذلك يدل على خطأ أشعث فيما رواه». وقال العلائي: «هذا لو كان أشعث مقاوماً لمن ذكر، فكيف وهو دونهم في الإتقان والحفظ بكثير وقد مس أيضاً، وهذا وحده كاف في رد زيادة التشهد». وقال ابن عبد البر: «أما التشهد في سجدتي السهو فلا أحفظه من وجه صحيح عن النبي». أثر هذه الزيادة في اختلاف الفقهاء اختلف الفقهاء في سجود السهو هل فيه تشهد وسلام أم لا؟ فذهب أنس بن مالك، والشعبي، والحسن، وعطاء إلى أنه لا تشهد ولا سلام في سجود السهو. في حين ذهب عمار بن ياسر، وسعد بن أبي وقاص رضي الله عنهما، وابن أبي ليلى إلى أن فيه تسليماً ولم يذكروا شيئاً عن التشهد. وذهب عبد الله بن مسعود، وقتادة، والحكم، وحماد، والنخعي، والزيدية، والظاهرية إلى أن في سجود السهو تشهداً وتسليماً، وهو مذهب أبي حنيفة إذ قال: «كل سهو وجب في الصلاة عن زيادة أو نقصان فإن الإمام إذا تشهد سلم ثم سجد سجدتي السهو ثم يتشهد ويسلم، وليس شيء من السهو يجب سجوده قبل السلام». وذهب مالك، والشافعي، وإسحاق، وأحمد، واختاره الشوكاني إلى أنه إذا سجد سجدتي السهو بعد السلام، فإنه يتشهد بعدها ويسلم، أما إذا سجد قبل السلام فلا تشهد عليه.
Chapter Three: Disagreement in Chain and Text. Regarding Hushaym ibn Bashir, all of them from Khalid al-Hadhdha', with it without mentioning the addition. Al-Bayhaqi said: 'Ash'ath al-Hamrani narrated it exclusively. Shu'bah, Wahib, Ibn 'Ulayyah, al-Thaqafi, Hushaym, Hammad ibn Zayd, Yazid ibn Zuray', and others narrated it from Khalid al-Hadhdha'; none of them mentioned what Ash'ath mentioned from him. Ayyub narrated from Muhammad who said: I was informed about 'Imran, and he mentioned the salām without the tashahhud. In the narration of Hushaym, he mentioned the tashahhud before the two prostrations, and that indicates Ash'ath's error in what he narrated.' Al-'Ala'i said: 'This is if Ash'ath were comparable to those mentioned, but how can he when he is far below them in precision and memory, and he also made a mistake? This alone suffices to reject the addition of tashahhud.' Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: 'As for the tashahhud in the two prostrations of forgetfulness, I do not know it from an authentic source from the Prophet.' The effect of this addition on the disagreement of jurists: The jurists disagreed about the prostration of forgetfulness: is there tashahhud and salām in it or not? Anas ibn Malik, al-Sha'bi, al-Hasan, and 'Ata' went that there is no tashahhud and no salām in the prostration of forgetfulness. Meanwhile, 'Ammar ibn Yasir, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Ibn Abi Layla went that there is salām in it and did not mention anything about tashahhud. 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, Qatada, al-Hakam, Hammad, al-Nakha'i, the Zaydis, and the Zahiris went that there is tashahhud and salām in the prostration of forgetfulness, and it is the school of Abu Hanifa, as he said: 'Every forgetfulness that necessitates prostration in prayer due to an addition or omission: the imam, when he makes tashahhud and salām, then prostrates the two prostrations of forgetfulness, then makes tashahhud and salām. Nothing of forgetfulness requires its prostration before salām.' Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ishaq, Ahmad, and al-Shawkani chose that if he prostrates the two prostrations of forgetfulness after salām, then he makes tashahhud after them and salām. But if he prostrates before salām, there is no tashahhud upon him.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a case study showing how a discrepancy in hadith transmission (sanad and matn) directly causes disagreement among jurists in a fiqh ruling. The issue is the addition (ziyāda) of tashahhud in the narration of the prostration of forgetfulness (sujūd al-sahw). Al-Bayhaqi, al-'Ala'i, and Ibn 'Abd al-Barr argue that the addition is unreliable because the reliable narrators do not mention it, and Ash'ath al-Hamrani is weaker. This textual criticism then grounds the fiqh dispute: some scholars reject tashahhud and salām entirely (Anas, etc.), others accept salām only ('Ammar, etc.), others accept both (Ibn Mas'ud, Abu Hanifa), and others make a distinction based on when the prostration occurs (Malik, Shafi'i). Note the Hanafi position: Abu Hanifa requires tashahhud and salām after the prostration of forgetfulness, regardless of whether the forgetfulness was due to increase or decrease. The passage follows the author's order: first the hadith dispute, then the resulting fiqh ikhtilāf. All examples and rulings are from classical Hanafi sources.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujūd al-sahw): Two prostrations performed to rectify forgetfulness in prayer.
تشهد (tashahhud): A specific invocation recited while sitting in prayer, including the testimony of faith.
زيادة (ziyāda): An additional wording in a hadith not found in other narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the tashahhud performed in sujūd al-sahw?
A) Before the two prostrations
B) After the two prostrations along with salām
C) It is not required
D) Only if the forgetfulness was due to an increase

Key Takeaway
The dispute over tashahhud in sujūd al-sahw is fundamentally rooted in hadith criticism, not mere opinion.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Evaluating an Addition in Hadith: The Case of al-Azdi's 'النهار'
Case study of a hadith with a disputed addition from al-Azdi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of ziyadat al-thiqah to a hadith with conflicting narrations and determine which version is preponderant.

Core Idea
When one narrator adds a word contradicting the majority, the addition is rejected.

In this passage, we examine a concrete example from Hanafi usul al-fiqh: a hadith from Ibn 'Umar about prayers performed in pairs. The narrator al-Azdi added the word 'النهار' (the day), while twenty-three other trustworthy narrators omitted it. The author's detailed listing of chains allows us to apply the rule of ziyadat al-thiqah and see how Hanafis resolve such textual disagreements. This lesson will train you to evaluate similar cases in your own research.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and the disputed addition
Locate the core hadith text: «الليل والنهار مثنى مثنى». Note that al-Azdi added 'النهار', making it 'الليل والنهار مثنى مثنى' with the word already present, but his version would emphasize 'النهار' as an addition.
The addition changes the emphasis but not the essential meaning; the key is whether it is authentic.
2
Collect all chains and note who adds/omits
The passage lists four narrators from Ibn 'Umar who omit the word: Anas ibn Sirin, Humayd ibn 'Abd al-Rahman, Sa'd ibn 'Ubayda, and Salim ibn 'Abd Allah. Each is cited with multiple sources. Count the number of narrators who omit versus the one who (al-Azdi) adds.
The abundance of narrators omitting the addition strengthens the majority version.
3
Apply the principle of majority and trustworthiness
In Hanafi usul, if a single trustworthy narrator (thiqah) adds wording that contradicts other trustworthy narrators, the addition is rejected (munkar) unless corroborated. Here, the majority of narrators are also thiqah, so al-Azdi's addition is not accepted.
This differs from the Shafi'i position, which generally accepts ziyadat al-thiqah unless clearly erroneous.
4
Determine the preponderant version
Based on the comparison, the preponderant version is without 'النهار'. The hadith is thus: «الليل والنهار مثنى مثنى», meaning that both night and day prayers are performed in pairs. The addition is considered a weakness in al-Azdi's narration.
This conclusion is reached by tarjih (preponderance) of the majority chains.
5
Derive the fiqh ruling
The hadith is used to support that optional prayers (nafl) are prayed two rak'ahs at a time. The rejected addition might have suggested a different ruling for daytime prayers, but the dominant version keeps the ruling general for both night and day.
The fiqh implication is minimal here, but the usul principle is critical for evaluating hadith-based evidence.

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on disagreement in chain and text presents a case study of a hadith from Ibn 'Umar regarding prayers performed in pairs (مثنى مثنى) during night and day. The author first lists numerous scholars who narrated the hadith without the word 'النهار'. He then highlights that the narrator al-Azdi added that word, contradicting the majority. By enumerating the narrators who omit 'النهار', the author demonstrates that the addition is a likely error or a weak variant. In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, such analysis is crucial: when a trustworthy narrator adds extra wording (زيادة الثقة) that contradicts other reliable narrators, the addition may be rejected unless corroborated. This passage shows the author's method of collecting multiple chains to establish the dominant wording. The extensive footnotes provide the exact references for each narration, allowing verification. The fiqh implication is that the hadith is typically used to prove that optional prayers (nafl) are performed two rak'ahs at a time, and the addition of 'النهار' might have suggested a different ruling for daytime prayers, but the majority version prevails.

Key Terms
زيادة الثقة (ziyadat al-thiqah): An addition made by a trustworthy narrator to a hadith text that is not found in other reliable narrations.
اختلاف السند والمتن (ikhtilaf al-sanad wa al-matn): Disagreement in the chain of narrators (sanad) or the text (matn) of a hadith that leads to differences in juristic rulings.
شاذ (shadhdh): A narration that is isolated and contradicts more reliable narrations; it is considered weak.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a trustworthy narrator adds a word that the majority of other trustworthy narrators omit, the addition is rejected, and the preponderant version is adopted.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Daytime Nafl Prayer: Two vs Four Rak'ahs
Dispute on daytime nafl prayer: two vs four rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on daytime nafl prayer, state the Hanafi position and its evidence, and evaluate the methodological disagreement between Hanafis and Shafi'is.

Core Idea
Daytime nafl can be four rak'ahs with one salam, based on a specific hadith that overrides general qiyas.

This passage examines a classical usul dispute: how should daytime supererogatory prayer be performed? The disagreement turns on whether a specific hadith (four rak'ahs before Dhuhr) should take precedence over a general analogy with night prayer. The Hanafi school, following Ibn Umar and Abu Hanifa, holds that daytime nafl is best prayed as four rak'ahs, while the Shafi'i school holds it should be two rak'ahs like the night prayer. You will learn to dissect the evidence each side uses and understand the thamarah (practical consequence) of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and list the scholars on each side. Note that the first opinion (two rak'ahs) is held by Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad, etc., while the second (four rak'ahs) is held by Abu Hanifa, Ibn Umar, etc.
2
Extract the evidence for each side
Find the specific hadith or analogy cited. The first side uses a general report equating day and night prayers. The second side uses the hadith of Abu Ayyub: 'Four before Dhuhr in which one does not give salam, for them the gates of heaven are opened.'
Note that the second side also allows praying two by two as an option, but prefers four.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing dalil
The Hanafi response, implied but not explicit in the text, is that the specific hadith overrides the general qiyas because it is direct and authentic. This reflects the Hanafi usul principle that a specific text (khass) takes precedence over general analogy (qiyas).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis pray the sunnah of Dhuhr as 4 rak'ahs with one salam (or two), while Shafi'is pray it as 2+2 rak'ahs with salam after each pair. This affects daily practice.
5
Evaluate the strength of the hadith of Abu Ayyub
The passage provides a long chain of narrators, showing the hadith is well-attested. However, it is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it cannot establish an obligatory ruling but can override a weaker qiyas.
Consider: does the hadith specifically refer to daytime nafl? It mentions 'before Dhuhr', which is a daytime nafl, so it applies directly.

أثر الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء كيف تصلى نافلة النهار؟
اختلف العلماء في نافلة النهار كيف تصلى على مذهبين:
المذهب الأول وهو أن تصلى مثنى مثنى، وهو ما ذهب إليه سعيد بن جبير والحسن البصري، وحماد بن أبي سليمان، ومالك، والشافعي، وأحمد، وداود، وابن المنذر. قال الشافعي - رحمه الله -: صلاة الليل والنهار من النافلة سواء يسلم في كل ركعتين، هكذا جاء الخبر عن النبي في صلاة الليل، وقد يروى عنه خبر يثبت أهل الحديث مثله في صلاة النهار، ولو لم يثبت كان إذ أمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في صلاة الليل أن يسلم من كل ركعتين كان معقولاً في الخبر عنه أنه أراد والله تعالى أعلم الفرق بين الفريضة والنافلة، ولا تختلف النافلة في الليل والنهار كما لا تختلف المكتوبة في الليل والنهار؛ لأنها موصولة كلها. وقال أيضاً: وهكذا ينبغي أن تكون النافلة في الليل والنهار.
المذهب الثاني: أنها تصلى أربعاً وهو ما ذهب إليه ابن عمر، وأبو حنيفة، إذ ذهب إلى أنه يصلى في نفل النهار أربعاً بتسليمة أو اثنتين، والأفضل أربع، والأوزاعي، وأبو يوسف، ومحمد، وإسحاق، واستدلوا بحديث أبي أيوب الأنصاري عن النبي أنه قال: «أربع قبل الظهر لا يسلم فيهن تفتح لهن أبواب السماء»، وأجاز ذلك أحمد، وقال ابن قدامة في المغني: وحديث أبي أيوب يرويه عبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عمر? (۱۰) مسائل أبي داود ۷۲ ومسائل عبد الله بن أحمد ٢٩٦/٢ والمقنع: ٣٤، والمغني ٧٦١/١
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. 396. The effect of hadith on the difference of jurists: How is the daytime supererogatory prayer performed? The scholars differed on how the daytime supererogatory prayer is performed into two schools: The first school: that it is performed two by two, which is what Sa'id ibn Jubayr, al-Hasan al-Basri, Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman, Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, Dawud, and Ibn al-Mundhir held. Al-Shafi'i said: The night and daytime supererogatory prayers are the same; one gives salam after every two rak'ahs. This is how the report came from the Prophet regarding the night prayer, and a report is narrated from him that hadith scholars affirm similar to it regarding the daytime prayer. And if it were not established, when the Messenger of Allah ordered in the night prayer to give salam after every two rak'ahs, it would be understood from the report about him that he intended, and Allah knows best, the difference between obligatory and supererogatory prayer, and the supererogatory prayer does not differ between night and day just as the obligatory prayer does not differ between night and day, because it is all continuous. He also said: And this is how the supererogatory prayer should be in night and day. The second school: that it is performed as four, which is what Ibn Umar, Abu Hanifa held, as he held that in the daytime supererogatory prayer one prays four rak'ahs with one salam or two, and the best is four, and al-Awza'i, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Ishaq. They used as evidence the hadith of Abu Ayyub al-Ansari from the Prophet that he said: 'Four before Dhuhr in which one does not give salam, for them the gates of heaven are opened.' Ahmad allowed that, and Ibn Qudamah said in al-Mughni: The hadith of Abu Ayyub is narrated by Ubayd Allah ibn Abd Allah ibn Umar? ... and it was reported by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani in al-Hujja 'ala Ahl al-Madina 1/272-273, and al-Tayalisi (597), and Abd al-Razzaq (4814), and al-Humaydi (385), and Ibn Abi Shayba (5940) and (5941), and Ahmad 5/416, 418, 419, and Abd ibn Humayd (226), and Abu Dawud (1270), and Ibn Majah (1157), and al-Tirmidhi in al-Shama'il (293) and (294) in our verification, and Ibn Khuzayma (1214) and (1215), and al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar 1/335, and Ibn Hibban in al-Thiqat 5/163-164, and al-Tabarani in al-Kabir (4031-4038), and al-Daraqutni in al-'Ilal 6/169, and Ibn 'Adi in al-Kamil 7/59, and al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak 3/461, and Tammam in his Fawa'id (380), and al-Bayhaqi 2/488-489, and al-Khatib in Muwaddih Awham al-Jam' wa al-Tafriq 1/168-169 from multiple chains from Abu Ayyub al-Ansari. The hadith has a witness from the hadith of Abd Allah ibn al-Sa'ib, reported by Ahmad 3/411, and al-Tirmidhi in al-Jami' al-Kabir (478), and in al-Shama'il (295) in our verification, and al-Nasa'i in al-Kubra (331), and al-Baghawi (890) with a sound chain. (10) Masa'il Abi Dawud: 72, and Masa'il Abd Allah ibn Ahmad 2/296-297, and al-Muqni': 34, and al-Mughni 1/761.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a dispute among scholars (ikhtilaf) regarding the manner of performing the daytime supererogatory prayer (nafl al-nahar). The author presents two main opinions. The first opinion, held by many early scholars including Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad, is that the daytime nafl is prayed two rak'ahs at a time, just like the night nafl. They base this on the general analogy between night and day prayers, and on a reported hadith equating them. The second opinion, held by Ibn Umar, Abu Hanifa, and his students, is that it is preferable to pray four rak'ahs with a single salam or two, citing the specific hadith of Abu Ayyub al-Ansari about four rak'ahs before Dhuhr. The text also notes that Ahmad ibn Hanbal allowed both methods. The passage then provides a detailed citation chain for the hadith of Abu Ayyub, showing its wide transmission. The commentary here should note that the Hanafi school follows the second opinion, preferring four rak'ahs for daytime nafl, while the Shafi'i school follows the first opinion of two rak'ahs. This difference stems from how each school weights the evidence: the Hanafis give precedence to the specific hadith over the general analogy.

Key Terms
نافلة النهار (nafl al-nahar): Supererogatory prayers performed during daytime, as opposed to night.
مثنى مثنى (mathna mathna): Two rak'ahs at a time, with a salam after each pair.
أربعاً بتسليمة (arba'an bi taslima): Four rak'ahs with one salam at the end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) 'The daytime prayer is like the night prayer.'

Key Takeaway
The dispute over daytime nafl prayer illustrates how a specific hadith (khass) can override a general analogy (qiyas) in Hanafi usul, leading to a different practice than other schools.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Daytime Nawafil: Two or Four Rak'ahs? A Usul Dispute
Fiqh dispute on nawafil rak'ah count based on hadith differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the hadith evidence in the dispute over daytime voluntary rak'ahs and apply the Hanafi method of tarjih based on narrator consistency and practice.

Core Idea
A single narrator's addition cannot override the majority; Ibn 'Umar's own practice confirms the four-rak'ah view.

This passage is a classic usul dispute: two madhhabs derive different rulings from the same raw material of hadith. The Shafi'i school applies a general command from night prayer to daytime by analogy, while the Hanafi school relies on a specific hadith (Abu Ayyub) and critiques the opposing hadith (al-Bariqi) for a single-narrator addition. By dissecting this dispute, you will learn how Hanafi usul evaluates hadith authenticity and resolves apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions and their core evidence
State the Shafi'i position (two rak'ahs per salutation, based on night prayer command) and the Hanafi position (four rak'ahs, based on the hadith of Abu Ayyub). Note the key hadiths involved.
The Shafi'i also cite the hadith of al-Bariqi adding 'daytime' to a general command.
2
Analyze the Hanafi's specific hadith (Abu Ayyub)
Examine the hadith 'Four before Zuhr without salutation...' – what does it explicitly command? Is its chain reliable? (Note: later commentators consider it weak, but the passage treats it as evidence.)
The Hanafi school adduces this hadith as direct proof for four rak'ahs.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i evidence – the general command and al-Bariqi hadith
The general command for night prayer is to pray two rak'ahs per salutation. Al-Bariqi narrated that the Prophet prayed four during the day with two salutations. Identify the weakness: al-Bariqi alone added the word 'daytime' among fifteen narrators.
The Hanafi critique focuses on this single-narrator addition.
4
Apply Hanafi critique: ziyadat al-thiqah
In Hanafi usul, an addition by a trustworthy narrator is accepted only if it does not contradict more reliable transmissions. Here, the majority of narrators from Ibn 'Umar did not mention 'daytime', so al-Bariqi's addition is rejected.
Also, Ibn 'Umar's own practice of praying four confirms the weakness of the contrary narration.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Determine which position is stronger based on the evidence. The Hanafi view relies on a specific hadith (Abu Ayyub) and is supported by the practice of a Companion (Ibn 'Umar). The Shafi'i view relies on analogy and a weak addition.
The passage indicates preference for the Hanafi view by pointing out the weakness of the opposing proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ركعتين، هكذا جاء الخبر عن النبي في صلاة الليل، وقد يروى عنه خبر يثبت أهل الحديث مثله في صلاة النهار، ولو لم يثبت كان إذ أمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في صلاة الليل أن يسلم من كل ركعتين كان معقولاً في الخبر عنه أنه أراد والله تعالى أعلم الفرق بين الفريضة والنافلة، ولا تختلف النافلة في الليل والنهار كما لا تختلف المكتوبة في الليل والنهار؛ لأنها موصولة كلها. وقال أيضاً: وهكذا ينبغي أن تكون النافلة في الليل والنهار. المذهب الثاني: أنها تصلى أربعاً وهو ما ذهب إليه ابن عمر، وأبو حنيفة، إذ ذهب إلى أنه يصلى في نفل النهار أربعاً بتسليمة أو اثنتين، والأفضل أربع، والأوزاعي، وأبو يوسف، ومحمد، وإسحاق، واستدلوا بحديث أبي أيوب الأنصاري عن النبي أنه قال: أربع قبل الظهر لا يسلم فيهن تفتح لهن أبواب السماء، وأجاز ذلك أحمد، وقال ابن قدامة في المغني: وحديث أبي أيوب يرويه عبيد الله بن معتب وهو ضعيف ومفهوم الحديث المتفق عليه يدل على جواز الأربع لا على تفضيلها، وأما حديث البارقي فإنه تفرد بزيادة لفظة النهار من بين سائر الرواة، وقد رواه عن ابن عمر نحو خمسة عشر نفساً لم يقل ذلك أحد سواه وكان ابن عمر يصلي أربعاً فيدل ذلك على ضعف روايته أو على أن المراد بذلك الفضيلة مع جواز غيره والله تعالى أعلم.
Two rak'ahs — thus the report came from the Prophet regarding night prayer, and a report is narrated from him which the people of hadith affirm similarly for daytime prayer. If it were not established, then when the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, commanded in night prayer to say the salutation after every two rak'ahs, it would be understood from the report that he intended, and Allah knows best, the distinction between the obligatory and the voluntary. And the voluntary prayer does not differ between night and day just as the obligatory prayer does not differ between night and day; because it is all uninterrupted. He also said: Thus should the voluntary prayer be in night and day. The second opinion: It is prayed as four, which is the view of Ibn 'Umar, Abu Hanifa, for he held that in the daytime voluntary prayer one prays four with one or two salutations, and the best is four, al-Awza'i, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Ishaq. They adduced the hadith of Abu Ayyub al-Ansari from the Prophet: 'Four before Zuhr without salutation, the doors of heaven are opened for them.' Ahmad permitted that. Ibn Qudamah said in al-Mughni: The hadith of Abu Ayyub is narrated by 'Ubayd Allah ibn Mu'attib, who is weak, and the understood meaning of the agreed-upon hadith indicates the permissibility of four, not its preference. As for the hadith of al-Bariqi, he alone added the word 'daytime' among all the narrators. About fifteen individuals narrated it from Ibn 'Umar, and no one else said that. Ibn 'Umar himself prayed four, which indicates the weakness of his narration or that what is intended by that is virtue along with permissibility of others. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the number of rak'ahs in voluntary (nafl) prayers during the day. The first opinion (Imam al-Shafi'i's) holds that all voluntary prayers, whether night or day, should be prayed in units of two rak'ahs, based on the general command of the Prophet for night prayer and the assumption that daytime is similar. The second opinion, held by the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and others like Ibn 'Umar, al-Awza'i, and Ishaq, is that daytime voluntary prayers are best performed in sets of four rak'ahs (with one or two salutations), based on the specific hadith of Abu Ayyub that states 'Four before Zuhr without salutation.' The Hanafis argue that the hadith of al-Bariqi, which reports the Prophet praying four during the day with two salutations, is weak because the narrator al-Bariqi alone added the word 'daytime' while other narrators from Ibn 'Umar did not, and Ibn 'Umar himself prayed four. The agreed-upon hadith (the one from Abu Ayyub) permits four, though it does not necessarily prefer it. Thus the Hanafi school holds that four is permissible and virtuous.

Key Terms
نافلة (nafilah): Voluntary prayer, supererogatory act of worship
مكتوبة (maktubah): Obligatory prayer (fard)
الشفع (al-shaf'): Pair of rak'ahs

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, how many rak'ahs are recommended for daytime voluntary prayers?
A) Two rak'ahs
B) Four rak'ahs
C) Eight rak'ahs
D) It is not specified

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes a specific hadith with a stronger chain and consistent practice over a general analogy, demonstrating the usul principle of evaluating evidence authenticity over logical extension.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Defining al-Shādh: The Contradiction of a Reliable Narrator
Defining shādh as a concept in hadith and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-shādh according to the settled Hanafi usul, identify when a narration is shādh, and distinguish it from other types of solitary narrations.

Core Idea
Shādh is not any lone narration; it is a reliable narrator contradicting a more reliable one.

In hadith criticism, not every solitary narration is rejected. The term 'shādh' has a precise technical meaning: a reliable narrator (thiqa) reports something that contradicts the narration of narrators who are more reliable in number or memory. This passage from a usul text lays out the definition, its linguistic roots, and the scholarly debate around it. Understanding shādh is crucial for evaluating hadith proofs in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
Examine the Arabic root sh-dh-dh: solitary, separate from the group. This gives the sense of being odd or deviant.
Linguistically, shādh applies to anything solitary, but technically it is restricted.
2
Grasp the technical definition
Read the passage's definition: a thiqa contradicts one who is awthaq (more reliable) either in number (density of narrators) or memory (ḥifẓ).
This is the settled definition according to Ibn Ḥajar.
3
Compare alternative definitions
Note al-Ḥākim's view (any solitary thiqa narration) and al-Khalīlī's view (any single-chain narration). Understand why the settled definition is preferred.
The key difference: the settled definition requires contradiction, not just solitude.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical impact: under al-Ḥākim's definition, many ṣaḥīḥ aḥād would be considered shādh; under the settled one, only contradictory narrations are shādh and thus not cited in fiqh.
This affects how many hadith are accepted as proofs.
5
Apply the concept to an example
Examine the hadith of Abū Qatādah on hunting while not in iḥrām. Maʿmar's version adds 'daytime', contradicting more reliable narrators. Identify why this addition is shādh.
This shows how the rule is used in practice.
