أسد الغابة ٣٧٤/٥ ، والإصابة ،٤ / ١٥٨ ، والتقريب (۸۳۱۱).
The third topic: The difference of a reliable narrator with other reliable narrators, and the effect of that on the disagreement of jurists. Its name: The differences occurring in the text or chain branch into multiple types, each type having its own ruling. Among those differences is that a reliable narrator contradicts other reliable narrators. Such contradiction varies: it may be from a reliable narrator contradicting another reliable narrator, or from a reliable narrator contradicting a number of reliable narrators. If the contradictor is one and not a group, then it is required that he be more reliable than the one with whom the difference occurs. This type of contradiction is called by scholars of terminology 'al-shādh' (the odd). It is: that a reliable narrator contradicts one who is more reliable than him, either in number or in memory. This definition is taken from al-Shāfiʿī's definition of al-shādh. It is narrated from Yūnus ibn ʿAbd al-Aʿlā: al-Shāfiʿī said to me: 'The shādh of hadith is not that a reliable narrator narrates what no one else narrates; rather, al-shādh is that a reliable narrator narrates a hadith that contradicts what the people have narrated.' ... And al-shādh linguistically means 'the solitary one'. It is said: shādda yashuddu/yashiddu – with dammah or kasrah on the shīn – i.e., he became alone apart from the majority. And 'the man shādda' means he isolated himself from his companions. Similarly, everything solitary is shādh. From that: 'it is shādh from analogy', 'this deviates from the principles', 'a deviant word', and so on. Thus: al-shudhūdh is the contradiction of a reliable narrator to one more reliable in memory or number. This is what has become settled in terminology. Al-Ḥāfiẓ Ibn Ḥajar said: 'The chosen interpretation of al-shādh is that it contradicts the narration of one who is more preponderant than him.' Then, the contradiction of a reliable narrator to other reliable narrators is natural, since narrators differ in the amount of their memory, vigilance, and precision from the time they receive hadith from their teachers until the time they deliver it. These variations in memory make the insightful critic distinguish between narrations, and distinguish the narration that is differed upon from that which is not, and the shādh from the preserved, and the known from the objectionable. Among the examples of a hadith of a reliable narrator contradicting a more reliable narrator is what Maʿmar ibn Rāshid narrated from Yaḥyā ibn Abī Kathīr, from ʿAbdullāh ibn Abī Qatādah, from his father, who said: 'I went out with the Messenger of Allah from al-Ḥudaybiyyah. My companions entered iḥrām while I did not. I saw a donkey, so I charged at it and hunted it. I mentioned the matter to the Messenger of Allah and mentioned that I had not...'
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of al-shādh (the odd/abnormal) in hadith and its impact on fiqh. It defines it as a reliable narrator (thiqa) contradicting one or more narrators who are more reliable (awthaq) either in number of narrators or in memory (ḥifẓ). This definition is attributed to Imam al-Shāfiʿī and is accepted by later scholars. The passage also mentions alternative definitions: al-Ḥākim al-Naysābūrī defined shādh as a hadith narrated only by a single thiqa without any follow-up (mutābiʿ), while Abū Yaʿlā al-Khalīlī said it is a hadith with only one chain, regardless of the narrator's reliability. However, the settled definition is the one given by Ibn Ḥajar: 'the narration that contradicts one that is more preponderant.' The passage gives an example of a shādh narration: Maʿmar ibn Rāshid's version of the hadith of Abū Qatādah about hunting a donkey while not in iḥrām. This narration includes the phrase 'in the daytime' which other narrators did not include; since Maʿmar was less reliable than other narrators, his addition is considered shādh and thus not accepted. The impact on fiqh is that such narrations cannot be used to establish rulings.

Key Terms
الشاذ (al-shādh): A hadith narrated by a reliable narrator that contradicts more reliable narrators in number or memory.
الثقة (al-thiqa): A reliable narrator who is trustworthy and has good memory.
الأوثق (al-awthaq): More reliable; a narrator superior in memory, number of narrators, or both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the settled definition of al-shādh according to the passage?
A) A hadith narrated only by a single trustworthy narrator without any follow-up.
B) A hadith that has only one chain of transmission, regardless of the narrator's reliability.
C) A reliable narrator contradicting one who is more reliable in number or memory.
D) Any narration that is rejected in fiqh.

Key Takeaway
A shādh narration is not simply a solitary one; it specifically contradicts stronger evidence and is thus rejected as a proof in fiqh.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Tarjih by Majority Narration in the Hadith of Abu Qatada
Analyzing the shudhudh of Ma'mar's narration over the majority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tarjih by kathrat al-turuq to identify a shadhdh narration and justify why the majority report is preferred.

Core Idea
When trustworthy narrators contradict a single one, the majority report (mahfuz) prevails; the solitary is shadhdh.

This passage presents a classic example of Hanafi hadith criticism: two authentic chains of the same incident conflict. The author, following Ibn Hazm, shows how a single narrator’s version is deemed odd (shadhdh) when multiple equally trustworthy narrators agree on the opposite. Understanding this method is vital for resolving contradictory ahadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting narrations
Recognize that there are two versions: the majority (Hisham, Ali, Muawiya, Shayban, plus others) report that the Prophet ate; Ma'mar alone reports he did not.
Both narrations are from Yahya ibn Abi Kathir.
2
Count the narrators and assess trustworthiness
List the narrators on each side. All are thiqah (trustworthy). The group consists of four narrators from one chain plus additional chains from other routes.
Trustworthiness is equal; the deciding factor is number.
3
Apply the Hanafi tarjih rule
The principle of kathrat al-turuq: the report of the majority (al-jama'ah) is given precedence over the solitary report because probability of error is lower in a collective transmission.
This rule is part of Hanafi usul al-hadith.
4
Conclude shudhudh
Ma'mar's narration is shadhdh (odd) and not acted upon; the majority version is mahfuz (preserved) and becomes the basis for action.
Shudhudh does not mean the narrator is weak, only that his report is outweighed.

الفصل الثالث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن
حازم بن دينار عن عبد الله بن أبي قتادة في هذا الحديث أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أكل منها، وتلك الرواية أودعها صاحبا الصحيح (١) كتابيهما دون رواية معمر وإن كان الإسنادان صحيحين. وقال ابن حزم: لا يخلو العمل في هذا ثلاثة أوجه إما أن تغلب رواية من الجماعة (٣) على رواية معمر لا سيما وفيهم من يذكر سماع يحيى من أبي قتادة، ولم يذكر معمرا، أو تسقط رواية يحيى بن أبي كثير جملة؛ لأنه اضطرب عليه)، ويؤخذ برواية أبي حازم وأبي محمد وابن موهب الذين لم يضطرب عليهم؛ لأنه لا يشك ذو حين أن إحدى الروايتين وهم، إذ لا يجوز أن تصح الرواية في أنه عليه السلام أكل منه، وتصح الرواية في أنه عليه السلام لم يأكل منه وهي قصة واحدة في وقت واحد في مكان واحد في صيد واحد. وسأشرح الآن شذوذ رواية معمر، فأقول: خالف - وهو ثقة - رواية الجمع عن يحيى، فقد رواه هشام الدستوائي معمر ثبت، وعلي بن المبارك ثقة، ومعاوية بن سلام - وهو ثقة -، وشيبان بن عبد الرحمن - وهو ثقة -، فهؤلاء أربعتهم رووه عن يحيى بن أبي كثير، ولم يذكروا هاتين اللفظتين. كما أن الحديث ورد من طريق عبد الله بن أبي قتادة من غير طريق يحيى بن أبي كثير، ولم تذكر فيه اللفظتان مما يؤكد ذلك شذوذ رواية معمر بتلك الزيادة؛ فَقَدْ رَوَاهُ عثمان بن عبد الله بن موهب - وهو ثقة -، وأبو حازم سلمة دينار - وهو ثقة -، وعبد العزيز بن رفيع - وهو ثقة -، وصالح بن أبي حسان - وهو صدوق -؛ فهؤلاء أربعتهم رووه عن عبد الله بن أبي قتادة، عن أبيه، ولم يذكروا هاتين اللفظتين، كما أن هذا الحديث روي من طرق أخرى عن أبي قتادة، وليس فيه هاتان اللفظتان فقد رواه نافع مولى أبي قتادة.
Chapter Three / Difference in Sanad and Matn
Hazim ibn Dinar from Abdullah ibn Abi Qatada in this hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ate from it, and that narration was included by the two authors of the Sahihs (1) in their books, excluding the narration of Ma'mar even though both chains are authentic. And Ibn Hazm said: The matter in this has no escape from three possibilities: either the narration of the group (3) is given precedence over the narration of Ma'mar, especially among them is one who mentions Yahya's hearing from Abu Qatada, and he did not mention Ma'mar; or the narration of Yahya ibn Abi Kathir is dropped entirely because he became confused; and the narration of Abu Hazim, Abu Muhammad, and Ibn Mawhib is taken, who did not become confused; because no sensible person doubts that one of the two narrations is mistaken, since it cannot be that the narration is authentic that he (peace be upon him) ate from it, and the narration is authentic that he (peace be upon him) did not eat from it, while it is one story at one time in one place about one hunt. And I will now explain the oddness of Ma'mar's narration, so I say: He contradicted - and he is trustworthy - the narration of the group from Yahya, for Hisham al-Dastawa'i, Ma'mar (who is reliable), Ali ibn al-Mubarak (trustworthy), Mu'awiya ibn Sallam (trustworthy), and Shayban ibn Abd al-Rahman (trustworthy) narrated it from Yahya ibn Abi Kathir, and they did not mention these two phrases. Just as the hadith came via Abdullah ibn Abi Qatada through other than Yahya ibn Abi Kathir, and the two phrases were not mentioned in it, which confirms the oddness of Ma'mar's narration with that addition. For it was narrated by Uthman ibn Abdullah ibn Mawhib (trustworthy), Abu Hazim Salama ibn Dinar (trustworthy), Abd al-Aziz ibn Rafi' (trustworthy), and Salih ibn Abi Hassan (truthful); these four narrated it from Abdullah ibn Abi Qatada from his father, and they did not mention these two phrases. Also this hadith was narrated through other chains from Abu Qatada, and these two phrases are not in it, for Nafi', the freed slave of Abu Qatada, narrated it.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is an extended case study in the principles of hadith criticism, specifically tarjih (preponderance) when narrations conflict. The issue is whether the Prophet (peace be upon him) ate from the game hunted by Abu Qatada. Two sound chains exist: one via Ma'mar stating he did not eat, and the majority via others stating he ate. The author refers to Ibn Hazm's analysis: the only valid possibilities are to prefer the majority (al-jama'ah) narration over Ma'mar's, or to drop Yahya's narration altogether due to confusion. Since the majority is greater in number and includes narrators who explicitly mention hearing from Abu Qatada, their version is preferred. Ma'mar's narration is thus shadhdh (odd). The author then lists numerous trustworthy narrators from different chains who all omit the two key phrases (indicating eating or not eating). This multiplicity of support confirms the shudhudh of Ma'mar's version. In Hanafi usul, such tarjih by kathrat al-turuq is a standard method: when a single trusted narrator contradicts a larger group, the group's narration is given precedence (mahfuz) and the solitary one is shadhdh (rejected). This principle directly affects fiqh rulings—here, the Hanafi position follows the majority that the Prophet ate, which impacts the permissibility of eating from game hunted by a muhrim? Actually the ruling is about the permissibility of eating from an animal hunted by a muhrim, but the text focuses on the hadith criticism aspect.

Key Terms
شذوذ (shudhūdh): The quality of a narration that contradicts a more reliable or numerous authority without possibility of reconciliation.
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Preferring one narration over another based on evidence such as number of narrators, reliability, or additional corroboration.
الجماعة (al-jamā‘ah): The group of narrators who transmit a hadith with consistency, as opposed to a single individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is Ma'mar's narration considered shadhdh in the passage?
A) Because Ma'mar is not trustworthy.
B) Because his report contradicts the Qur'an.
C) Because many trustworthy narrators from multiple chains report the opposite, and his single report cannot override them.
D) Because the hadith has a broken chain.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a trustworthy narrator contradicts a larger group of equally trustworthy narrators, the group's report prevails (mahfuz) and the solitary is rejected as shadhdh.
